China: Agency (1950-1960)

Christian or Pro-Christian Leaders

  1. Policies on the Withdrawal of Missionaries from Enemy Nations (Yang Weihua 2020: 91-93; Zhang Le 2020: 114-120)

On July 2, 1941, China formally severed diplomatic relations with Germany and Italy. Both governments planned to evacuate their citizens from China, but the majority of German and Italian missionaries insisted on remaining in the country. In August, Foreign Minister Guo Taichi stated that German and Italian missionaries could continue to reside in China as long as they focused on their missionary activities and complied with Chinese laws. However, local military and political authorities were dissatisfied with the Foreign Ministry‚Äôs stance and demanded the removal of all such foreigners to prevent them from acting as spies.

On December 9, China declared war on Germany and Italy, categorizing the remaining German and Italian missionaries as enemy aliens. The Foreign Ministry issued orders to keep these foreigners under temporary protection and surveillance, allowing them to stay in their original locations while monitoring their communications. Nonetheless, calls for more stringent measures began to emerge. To establish norms and unify perspectives, the Foreign Ministry consulted with various departments and initially determined that missionaries should not receive any shelter but be concentrated in designated churches under local supervision to continue their religious work. The Military Commission emphasized that several espionage cases disguised as missionary work had already been uncovered, making the justification for continued missionary activities questionable. Nevertheless, the Foreign Ministry insisted on preserving the status quo, emphasizing that the Vatican and foreign missionary organizations had greatly assisted in wartime efforts and propaganda, and due to the relatively lenient treatment by Germany and Italy toward their nationals, concentrated oversight would be sufficient to mitigate espionage concerns.

In January 1942, the Foreign Ministry published the ‚ÄúRegulations on the Treatment of Enemy Nationals‚Äù and the ‚ÄúRegulations on the Concentration and Surveillance of Enemy National Clergy.‚Äù Article 13 stipulated that enemy clergy should be concentrated in designated Catholic or locally led Jesus churches while continuing their missionary work under the supervision of local authorities. The guidelines emphasized that clergy would be stationed in designated churches for evangelistic endeavors, in keeping with the established directives.

In June 1942, after establishing diplomatic relations with the Vatican, Chiang Kai-shek proposed three principles for handling enemy national clergy: (1) Those suspected of espionage would be treated as enemy aliens; (2) Those without suspicion could freely preach within designated areas but were prohibited from entering military zones; (3) Church properties that needed to be returned would be handled accordingly unless proven to be hostile. The Executive Yuan issued a directive to implement these principles on June 25.

  1. Missionaries Detained in Internment Camps During the Occupation (Leck 2006; Zhang Qinying 2020: 63-109)

Following the outbreak of the Pacific War, American and British missionaries in China‚Äôs occupied regions were classified as enemy aliens and confined to various internment camps (Civilian Internment Camps).

The main detention centers included: Shenyang, Beiping, Shandong (Weixian, Qingdao), Shanghai (Pudong, Longhua, Zhabei, Lincoln Road, Daxilu, the Cathedral, Shanghai West, Xujiahui, Franciscan Church, Yuyuan Road, and more than ten other locations), and Guangzhou.

Statistics reveal that during World War II, the number of Western civilians interned by the Japanese military in Asia was roughly comparable to that of prisoners of war, totaling approximately 130,000 individuals. Among these, around 9,350 Western civilians were detained in China. Scholars have pointed out that because expatriates were not military POWs, internment camps should not be equated with POW camps or compared to Nazi Germany‚Äôs concentration camps. If American and British missionaries did not evacuate from China, they too would be interned. It is generally believed that the number of missionaries detained in Chinese internment camps was approximately 1,200.

Some of these detention centers were located within areas associated with Christian and Catholic organizations. For instance, nearly a thousand individuals were held at the Weixian internment camp, associated with the Courtyard of the Happy Way operated by the Presbyterian Church in North America, which served as a grouping of church buildings that included a church, hospital, and school. The Stanley internment camp in Hong Kong was situated within the premises of the Anglican St. Stephen‚Äôs College. Detention centers such as the Cathedral in Xujiahui, the Jesuit Theological Institute, and the Franciscan Church in Shanghai were also locations for imprisoning missionaries and clergy.

Notable missionaries detained at the Weixian internment camp included Watson M. Hayes, President of North China Theological Seminary; Eric Liddell, the British Olympic champion from the 1924 games and a teacher at Tianjin New Learning School, who died in the camp on February 21, 1945; Father Raymond J. de Jaegher; and L. Y. Davies, academic dean at Shandong University. Ralph A. Ward, Bishop of the American Methodist Church, was also interned in Shanghai and Beiping. John L. Stuart, President of Yenching University, was imprisoned in Beiping.

  1. The Imprisonment of Chinese Theologian Zhao Zichen (Xing Fuzeng 2005: 286-289)

On December 8, 1941, the Japanese military attacked Pearl Harbor, marking the beginning of the Pacific War. Due to hostilities between Japan and the United States, Christian schools, hospitals, and organizations throughout North China were blockaded by Japanese forces. All British and American-run primary and secondary schools in Beiping were taken over by the Japanese military. The circumstances surrounding Yenching University were dire as well; just hours after the Pearl Harbor incident, Japanese troops surrounded the campus, capturing 11 professors, including Zhao Zichen, Zhang Dongxun, Lu Zhiwei, and Hong Weilian.

While imprisoned, Zhao resolved that upon his release he would accomplish three goals: first, to recuperate and restore his health; second, to write books; and third, to ‚Äúhelp the church achieve self-sufficiency and independence, transforming it into a church that belongs to China and does not rely on outsiders.‚Äù Despite enduring great suffering during his imprisonment, his faith became a source of strength, with prayer serving as his greatest source of empowerment. Simultaneously, he provided comfort to fellow prisoners through his faith.

Zhao was imprisoned until June 18, 1942, totaling 193 days in confinement. Following his release, he was sheltered by Ling Xianyang, president of the North China Anglican Church, residing within the Anglican compound in Beiping. During this time, Zhao completed manuscripts for ‚ÄúChristian Progress‚Äù and ‚ÄúThe Life of St. Paul.‚Äù Later, he documented his experiences in prison in ‚ÄúMemoirs of Imprisonment,‚Äù published in 1948.

  1. The Communist Party‚Äôs Religious Policies During the War of Resistance (Wang Jianlang, Huang Kewu 2021: 941-942; Central Committee 1992a: 316; Chen Jinlong 2006: 74)

In May 1941, Mao Zedong drafted the ‚ÄúGovernance Guidelines for the Shaanxi-Gansu-Ningxia Border Region,‚Äù which stated: ‚ÄúProtect the religious freedom of all anti-Japanese citizens (landlords, capitalists, farmers, workers, etc.) and respect the religious beliefs and customs of the Mongolian and Hui ethnic groups.‚Äù When explaining this article, the border region government clarified that ‚Äúreligion refers to Islam, Lamaism, Catholicism, etc.‚Äù ‚ÄúWeakened ethnic groups and those believing in religion all have rights, and no one can restrict anyone else.‚Äù

The ‚ÄúTemporary Regulations on Land Use in the Jin-Ji-Lu-Yu Border Region,‚Äù published in 1941, stipulated: ‚ÄúLand contracts obtained lawfully by Catholic and Protestant churches shall be recognized as owned by the church; foreign missionaries who purchased or donated land in a personal capacity shall arrange for the ownership of that land to be transferred to the relevant church or school.‚Äù In January 1942, the Communist Party issued attachments to the ‚ÄúCentral Committee‚Äôs Decision on Land Policy in Anti-Japanese Base Areas,‚Äù stating: ‚ÄúReligious land (including land owned by Christian, Buddhist, Islamic, Taoist, and other sects) shall remain unchanged.‚ÄùIn the winter of 1934, Liu Liangmo organized a mass singing event in Shanghai under the YMCA and initiated the movement for songs of national salvation, composing a song titled ‚ÄúSave China.‚Äù In 1935, he collaborated with Lu Gancheng, the general secretary of the Shanghai YMCA, to establish a singing group within the organization. In May, he recorded ‚ÄúThe March of the Volunteers‚Äù on vinyl, which became an important piece performed by the singing group. When the ‚ÄúSeven Gentlemen‚Äù like Shen Junru were arrested by the government, Liu also participated in rescue efforts. In January 1936, the ‚ÄúShanghai United National Salvation Federation‚Äù was established, with Shanghai Christians issuing a declaration opposing passive diplomacy and demanding the use of force to uphold national integrity and dignity, of which Liu was one of the 28 initiators. In May, the ‚ÄúNational United Salvation Federation‚Äù was formed in Shanghai, and Liu served as a board member. During various activities organized by the Federation, Liu Liangmo led the singing of ‚ÄúThe March of the Volunteers,‚Äù earning him the title of ‚Äúcheerleader for the Salvation Federation‚Äù from Shen Junru and others. In June 1936, during the first anniversary of the establishment of the National United Salvation Federation, the mass singing group held a large singing event at the Shanghai Public Gymnasium, where Liu conducted from the stage.

In the summer of 1936, Liu traveled to the United States to attend the ‚ÄúWorld Student Christian Federation‚Äù conference held in San Francisco. After returning to China, he joined the YMCA‚Äôs ‚ÄúMilitary Services Department,‚Äù working in various places such as Suiyuan, Suzhou, Zhengzhou, and Changsha to promote services for soldiers. During this time, he authored ‚ÄúThe ABCs of Mass Singing,‚Äù which detailed how to teach the masses to sing and organize community singing groups.

In 1939, while in Jinhua, Zhejiang, he met with Zhou Enlai. Later, Liu was briefly detained by the Nationalist Party in Ningbo under suspicions of being a Communist but was released and subsequently applied to the YMCA to continue his studies in the United States.

From the summer of 1940 until August 1949, Liu Liangmo was primarily active in the United States, studying at Crozer Theological Seminary and the University of Pennsylvania. During this time, he also campaigned for China‚Äôs anti-Japanese efforts and organized singing groups among the expatriate community. After the victory in the anti-Japanese war, he continued to stay in the U.S., preparing to establish the American branch of the ‚ÄúChina Democratic League.‚Äù In September 1946, when Feng Yuxiang spoke in the U.S., Liu served as his translator.

In August 1949, Liu Liangmo received a telegram from Zhou Enlai via Gong Peng, inviting him to Beijing to participate in the new Political Consultative Conference. He promptly returned to China with his family. Along with Wu Yaozong, Deng Yuzhi, Zhang Xueyan, and Zhao Zichen, Liu became a Christian representative in the Political Consultative Conference. Subsequently, Liu offered his position to an Islamic representative, changing to an alternate representative. During the conference, Liu and several representatives jointly proposed that ‚ÄúThe March of the Volunteers‚Äù be adopted as the national anthem, which was approved.

After returning to China, he became the director of the national council‚Äôs work group within the YMCA and participated in a Christian visitation team in North China, meeting with Zhou Enlai in May 1950. Within the Communist Party, Wu Yaozong‚Äôs initiated Three-Self Reform Declaration was referred to as the ‚ÄúWu-Liu Movement,‚Äù highlighting Liu‚Äôs involvement.

Liu Liangmo played a significant role in the Three-Self Reform Movement: he was an initiator of the reform declaration and a member of the presidium for the ‚Äúmeeting of Christian groups accepting U.S. subsidies.‚Äù As an executive committee member, he was also the leader of the propaganda group. In his role as head of propaganda, he became a key figure in advocating for the Christian accusation movement. In May 1951, he published an article in the ‚ÄúPeople‚Äôs Daily‚Äù titled ‚ÄúHow to Hold a Church Complaint Meeting?‚Äù calling for local churches to understand that the purpose of the complaints was to eliminate imperialist influences within Christianity. The targets of the complaints were ‚Äúimperialist elements hidden within the church, their accomplices, and the failures of other churches.‚Äù He further provided guidance on organizing complaint meetings: first, establish a ‚ÄúComplaint Committee‚Äù to determine whom to accuse and who should be invited to testify; then hold mobilization meetings to instill the rationale and importance of why and what to accuse. Following this, accusation group meetings should be held in various churches and communities to identify the most effective accusers, inviting them to participate in the larger accusation meeting. He emphasized that during the preparation of complaints, guidance from the Religious Affairs Office was essential. In conjunction with the complaints, effective accusation statements could be published in local newspapers, and exhibitions could be held. Liu Liangmo‚Äôs guidance indeed became a reference model for various localities.

Moreover, he was an important theorist behind the ‚Äúcomplaint‚Äù movement. In the early phase of the reform movement, he published several articles in ‚ÄúTianfeng,‚Äù focusing on points such as: (1) American imperialism‚Äôs use of Christianity to invade China; (2) Opposition to imperialism is not contradictory to patriotism; (3) Critiques of ‚Äúsuper-political‚Äù stances and ‚Äúexclusive love‚Äù ideologies.In January 1948, the Executive Yuan amended Article 7 to state: ‚ÄúNationals, companies, or churches from countries that have entered into equitable treaties with our country may apply for registration and reissuance of land ownership certificates for land acquired in the name of Chinese nationals prior to the treaty, provided they possess valid documentation proving their rights as genuine purchasers at the time of acquisition and are endorsed by their country‚Äôs consulate.‚Äù

  1. The Religious Policies of the Communist Party During the Chinese Civil War (Zhonggong Zhongyang 1991: 264; 1992b: 35-36, 38; 1992c: 44-48)

In September 1945, the Central Committee of the Communist Party of China indicated in its ‚ÄúGuidance on the Work in Newly Liberated Cities‚Äù that ‚Äúthe rescue of allied prisoners and the protection of foreign nationals‚Ä¶ should not infringe upon religious organizations and charitable institutions, and we should strive not to commandeer schools‚Ä¶‚Äù

In February 1948, the Central Committee issued directives related to the treatment of foreigners, stating that ‚Äúin the newly occupied areas and cities, we will encounter more foreign expatriates and the churches, schools, hospitals, orphanages, and nursing homes established by them‚Ä¶ Generally, we will not adopt exclusionary or confiscatory policies toward these economic, cultural, and religious institutions established by foreign nationals, regardless of whether they belong to an imperialist nature.‚Äù It continued, ‚ÄúAs long as they acknowledge and comply with the laws and regulations of our democratic government and the People‚Äôs Liberation Army, they may be allowed to continue residing in our liberated areas, conduct business or other legitimate activities, and will be protected by our democratic government.‚Äù

Article 1 stated, ‚ÄúAny churches established by foreigners and the schools, hospitals, orphanages, and nursing homes they operate shall not be confiscated or destroyed after our army arrives. They are allowed to continue their activities as long as they comply with the laws of our armed forces and democratic government and do not engage in espionage or sabotage. Should any foreigners or agents entrusted by them flee, their properties will be put under government custody. However, the original organizers can return to take over. If these organizations have acquired land in rural areas or have occupied land, that land should be handled in accordance with land laws. However, any small gardens within the scope of these organizations should remain under their ownership, and property not attached to land should not be confiscated.‚Äù

Article 6 mentioned the need to ‚Äúidentify any imperialist elements among foreign nationals and pay attention to any conspiratorial sabotage actions‚Ä¶ When certain foreign missionaries engage in espionage or sabotage activities, we should not only detain and punish them but also gather sufficient evidence to publicize their misdeeds domestically and internationally, making a clear distinction between their criminal acts and legitimate religious activities. Those missionaries who are guilty should be prosecuted or expelled, but churches need not be closed. It is permissible for them to send other representatives to lead their churches to avoid misunderstandings by foreigners regarding our government‚Äôs stance on religion.‚Äù

In January 1949, the ‚ÄúGuidance on Foreign Affairs‚Äù issued by the Central Committee stated that ‚Äúin principle, the privileges of imperialism in China must be abolished and the independence of the Chinese nation must be realized, a position that is unwavering. However, in the execution steps, issues should be handled according to their nature and circumstances. Problems that are beneficial and resolvable for the Chinese people should be addressed. Those that remain unresolved should be deferred. Issues that are not significantly harmful or not considerably harmful to the Chinese people should not be rushed to resolve, even if they present easy solutions. Problems that have not been thoroughly studied or have not matured for resolution should also not be hastily approached.‚Äù Specifically regarding foreign missionaries, point 5 stated: ‚ÄúThose already in our liberated areas are allowed to continue residing and conducting their business, while newcomers are not permitted for the time being.‚Äù Point 6 concerning schools operated by foreigners specified: ‚ÄúExisting private foreign schools can maintain their status; their curricula must comply with the regulations applicable to other schools. New applications for establishment are denied.‚Äù Point 7 concerning hospitals operated by foreigners indicated: ‚ÄúExisting institutions may continue but will be subject to supervision and inspection. New establishments are not allowed without approval.‚Äù Point 10 regarding foreign charitable organizations specified: ‚ÄúThose belonging to imperialist governments‚Ä¶ should not be recognized, and their assistance and relief should be refused. Those belonging to international cooperative organizations should be evaluated based on whether they undermine our national sovereignty and the independence and autonomy of our populace before a decision is made to accept their aid.‚ÄùIn January 1948, the Executive Yuan amended Article 7 to state: ‚ÄúNationals, companies, or churches from countries that have entered into equitable treaties with our country may apply for registration and reissuance of land ownership certificates for land acquired in the name of Chinese nationals before the treaty, provided they possess valid documentation proving their rights as genuine purchasers at the time of acquisition and are endorsed by their country‚Äôs consulate.‚Äù

  1. The Religious Policies of the Communist Party During the Chinese Civil War (Zhonggong Zhongyang 1991: 264; 1992b: 35-36, 38; 1992c: 44-48)

In September 1945, the Central Committee of the Communist Party of China indicated in its ‚ÄúGuidance on Work in Newly Liberated Cities‚Äù that ‚Äúthe rescue of allied prisoners and the protection of foreign nationals‚Ä¶ should not infringe upon religious organizations and charitable institutions, and we should strive not to commandeer schools‚Ä¶‚Äù

In February 1948, the Central Committee issued directives regarding the treatment of foreigners, stating that ‚Äúin the newly occupied areas and cities, we will encounter more foreign expatriates and the churches, schools, hospitals, orphanages, and nursing homes established by them‚Ä¶ Generally, we will not adopt exclusionary or confiscatory policies regarding these economic, cultural, and religious institutions established by foreign nationals, regardless of whether they belong to an imperialist nature.‚Äù It continued, ‚ÄúAs long as they acknowledge and comply with the laws and regulations of our democratic government and the People‚Äôs Liberation Army, they may be permitted to continue residing in our liberated areas, conducting business or other legitimate activities, and will be protected by our democratic government.‚Äù

Article 1 specified that ‚Äúany churches established by foreigners and the schools, hospitals, orphanages, and nursing homes they operate shall not be confiscated or destroyed after our army arrives. They are allowed to continue their activities as long as they comply with the laws of our armed forces and democratic government and do not engage in espionage or sabotage. Should any foreigners or agents entrusted by them flee, their properties will be put under government custody. However, the original organizers can return to take over. If these organizations have acquired land in rural areas or have occupied land, that land should be handled in accordance with land laws. However, any small gardens within the scope of these organizations should remain under their ownership, and property not related to the land should not be confiscated.‚Äù

Article 6 mentioned the need to ‚Äúidentify any imperialist elements among foreign nationals and pay attention to any conspiratorial sabotage actions‚Ä¶ When certain foreign missionaries engage in espionage or sabotage activities, we should not only detain and punish them but also gather sufficient evidence to publicize their actions both domestically and internationally, making a clear distinction between their criminal activities and legitimate religious activities. Those missionaries found guilty must be prosecuted or expelled, but churches need not be closed. It is permissible for them to send other representatives to lead their churches to avoid misunderstandings by foreigners regarding our government‚Äôs stance on religion.‚Äù

In January 1949, the ‚ÄúGuidance on Foreign Affairs‚Äù issued by the Central Committee stated that ‚Äúin principle, the privileges of imperialism in China must be abolished and the independence of the Chinese nation must be achieved, a position that is unwavering. However, the sequence of actions should depend on the nature and circumstances of the issues. Problems that are beneficial and resolvable for the Chinese people should be tackled. Those that cannot be resolved should be postponed. Issues that pose no significant threat or harm to the Chinese people need not be rushed to resolve, even if they are easily solvable. Problems that have not been thoroughly researched or are not yet ripe for resolution should not be hastily addressed.‚Äù Specifically regarding foreign missionaries, point 5 stated: ‚ÄúThose already in our liberated areas are permitted to continue residing and conducting business, while newcomers are not permitted for now.‚Äù Point 6 concerning schools operated by foreigners specified: ‚ÄúExisting private foreign schools can maintain their status, and their curricula must comply with regulations applicable to other schools. New applications for establishment are denied.‚Äù Point 7, concerning hospitals operated by foreigners, indicated: ‚ÄúExisting institutions are allowed to continue but must be subject to our supervision and inspection. New establishments are not permitted without approval.‚Äù Point 10 concerning foreign charitable organizations specified: ‚ÄúThose associated with imperialist governments‚Ä¶ should not be recognized, and their assistance and relief should be refused. Those backed by international cooperative organizations should be evaluated based on whether they undermine our national sovereignty and the independence of our people before a decision can be made on whether to accept their assistance.‚Äù In January 1948, the Executive Yuan amended Article 7 to state: ‚ÄúNationals, companies, or churches from countries that have entered into equitable treaties with our country may apply for registration and reissuance of land ownership certificates for land acquired in the name of Chinese nationals before the treaty, provided they possess valid documentation proving their rights as genuine purchasers at the time of acquisition and are endorsed by their country‚Äôs consulate.‚Äù

  1. The Religious Policies of the Communist Party During the Chinese Civil War (Zhonggong Zhongyang 1991: 264; 1992b: 35-36, 38; 1992c: 44-48)

In September 1945, the Central Committee of the Communist Party of China indicated in its ‚ÄúGuidance on Work in Newly Liberated Cities‚Äù that ‚Äúthe rescue of allied prisoners and the protection of foreign nationals‚Ä¶ should not infringe upon religious organizations and charitable institutions, and we should strive not to commandeer schools‚Ä¶‚Äù

In February 1948, the Central Committee issued directives regarding the treatment of foreigners, stating that ‚Äúin the newly occupied areas and cities, we will encounter more foreign expatriates and the churches, schools, hospitals, orphanages, and nursing homes established by them‚Ä¶ Generally, we will not adopt exclusionary or confiscatory policies regarding these economic, cultural, and religious institutions established by foreign nationals, regardless of whether they belong to an imperialist nature.‚Äù It continued, ‚ÄúAs long as they acknowledge and comply with the laws and regulations of our democratic government and the People‚Äôs Liberation Army, they may continue residing in our liberated areas, conduct business or other legitimate activities, and will be protected by our democratic government.‚Äù

Article 1 specified that ‚Äúchurches established by foreigners and the schools, hospitals, orphanages, and nursing homes they operate shall not be confiscated or destroyed after our army arrives. They are allowed to continue their work as long as they comply with the laws of our armed forces and democratic government and do not engage in espionage or sabotage. If any foreigners or their agents flee, their properties will be placed under government custody, but the original organizers can return to take over the administration. If land has been acquired in rural areas or has been taken by force, that land will be handled according to land laws, but any small gardens associated with these organizations must remain under their ownership, and any property not attached to land shall not be confiscated.‚Äù

Article 6 discussed the need to ‚Äúidentify certain imperialist elements among foreign nationals and pay attention to any conspiring actions‚Ä¶ If certain foreign missionaries engage in espionage or sabotage activities, we should detain them and gather sufficient evidence to publicize their actions, distinguishing between these criminal acts and legitimate religious activities. Guilty missionaries should face prosecution or expulsion, while the churches need not be closed. It is acceptable for them to appoint other representatives to lead their churches to prevent misunderstandings by foreigners regarding our government‚Äôs stance on religion.‚Äù

In January 1949, the ‚ÄúGuidance on Foreign Affairs‚Äù issued by the Central Committee stated that ‚Äúin principle, the privileges of imperialism in China must be abolished, and the independence of the Chinese nation must be realized, which is a steadfast position. However, actions should be handled according to the nature and circumstances of the issues. Problems that benefit the Chinese people and can be resolved should be addressed. Those that cannot be resolved should be postponed. Issues that are not harmful or present minimal harm to the Chinese people, even if easily resolved, need not be rushed into resolution. Problems that have not been thoroughly studied or are not yet ripe for resolution should also not be hastily approached.‚ÄùIn the winter of 1934, Liu Liangmo organized a mass singing event in the Shanghai YMCA, initiating the national salvation song movement and composing a song titled ‚ÄúSave China.‚Äù In 1935, he worked with Lu Gancheng, the general secretary of the Shanghai YMCA, to establish a singing team within the organization. In May, he recorded ‚ÄúThe March of the Volunteers‚Äù onto vinyl, which became an important piece for the singing group. When the ‚ÄúSeven Gentlemen,‚Äù including Shen Junru, were arrested by the government, Liu participated in their rescue efforts. In January 1936, the ‚ÄúShanghai United National Salvation Federation‚Äù was formed, with Liu as one of the 28 initiators. In May, the ‚ÄúNational United Salvation Federation‚Äù was established in Shanghai, and Liu served as a board member. During various activities organized by the federation, Liu Liangmo led the singing of ‚ÄúThe March of the Volunteers,‚Äù earning him the title of ‚Äúcheerleader for the Salvation Federation‚Äù from Shen Junru and others. In June 1936, during the first anniversary of the National United Salvation Federation, the mass singing event was held at the Shanghai Public Gymnasium, where Liu conducted from the stage.

In the summer of 1936, Liu traveled to the United States to attend the ‚ÄúWorld Student Christian Federation‚Äù conference held in San Francisco. Upon returning to China, he joined the YMCA‚Äôs Military Services Department and worked in several locations including Suiyuan, Suzhou, Zhengzhou, and Changsha to promote services for soldiers. During this time, he authored ‚ÄúThe ABCs of Mass Singing,‚Äù detailing how to teach the public to sing and organize community singing groups.

In 1939, while in Jinhua, Zhejiang, he met with Zhou Enlai. Later, Liu was briefly detained by the Nationalist Party in Ningbo under suspicions of being a Communist, but after being released, he applied to the YMCA to further his studies in the United States.

From the summer of 1940 until August 1949, Liu was primarily active in the United States, studying at Crozer Theological Seminary and the University of Pennsylvania. During this period, he promoted China‚Äôs anti-Japanese efforts and organized singing groups among the expatriate community. After the victory in the anti-Japanese war, he remained in the U.S., working to establish the American branch of the ‚ÄúChina Democratic League.‚Äù In September 1946, when Feng Yuxiang gave a lecture in the U.S., Liu served as his translator.

In August 1949, Liu Liangmo received a telegram from Zhou Enlai via Gong Peng, inviting him to Beijing to attend the new Political Consultative Conference. He immediately returned to China with his family. Along with Wu Yaozong, Deng Yuzhi, Zhang Xueyan, and Zhao Zichen, he became a Christian representative in the Political Consultative Conference. Later, Liu offered his position to an Islamic representative, becoming an alternate representative instead. During the conference, he and several other representatives jointly proposed to adopt ‚ÄúThe March of the Volunteers‚Äù as the national anthem, which was subsequently approved.

After returning to China, Liu served as the director of the national council‚Äôs work group within the YMCA and participated in a Christian visitation team in North China, meeting with Zhou Enlai in May 1950. Within the Communist Party, Wu Yaozong‚Äôs initiated Three-Self Reform Declaration was referred to as the ‚ÄúWu-Liu Movement,‚Äù highlighting Liu‚Äôs involvement.

Liu Liangmo played a pivotal role in the Three-Self Reform Movement, being an initiator of the reform declaration and a member of the presidium for the ‚Äúmeeting of Christian groups accepting U.S. subsidies.‚Äù As a key figure in the propaganda initiative, he was vital in promoting the Christian accusation movement. In May 1951, he published an article in the ‚ÄúPeople‚Äôs Daily‚Äù titled ‚ÄúHow to Hold a Church Complaint Meeting?‚Äù urging local churches to understand that the purpose of the complaints was to eliminate imperialist influences within Christianity. The targets of the complaints focused on ‚Äúimperialist elements hidden within the church, their accomplices, and the failures of other churches.‚Äù He provided guidance on organizing complaint meetings: first, establish a ‚ÄúComplaint Committee‚Äù to determine whom to accuse and who should be invited to testify; then hold mobilization meetings to instill the rationale for the accusations. Following this, accusation group meetings should be held in various churches to identify the most effective accusers, inviting them to participate in a larger gathering. He emphasized that throughout the preparation process, guidance from the Religious Affairs Office was essential, and effective accusation statements should be published in local newspapers while organizing exhibitions. Liu Liangmo‚Äôs guidance became a reference model for various localities.

Moreover, he was a significant theorist of the ‚Äúcomplaint‚Äù initiative. During the early phase of the reform movement, he published several articles in ‚ÄúTianfeng,‚Äù focusing on points such as: (1) American imperialism‚Äôs use of Christianity to invade China; (2) Opposition to imperialism is not contradictory to patriotism; (3) Critiques of ‚Äúsuper-political‚Äù stances and ‚Äúexclusive love‚Äù ideologies.

Liu Liangmo‚Äôs journey reflects the complex interplay of religious faith, political ideals, and cultural movements during a tumultuous period in Chinese history. His engagement in notable movements such as the anti-Japanese war effort and the eventual embrace of reformist ideologies showcases his adaptability and dedication to both his faith and national identity.In the winter of 1934, Liu Liangmo organized a mass singing event in the Shanghai YMCA, initiating the national salvation song movement and composing a song titled ‚ÄúSave China.‚Äù In 1935, he collaborated with Lu Gancheng, the general secretary of the Shanghai YMCA, to establish a singing group within the organization. His commitment to both his faith and his nation showcases his adaptability and dedication, highlighting his significant influence on the Christian community in China.Tu Yuqing (Y. C. Tu, 1895-1975) was born in Hubei HanYang and came from a Christian family, having been baptized at a young age. His grandfather, Tu Songshan, was a minister in the Methodist Church. During his primary and junior high school years, he studied in church schools in HanYang. In 1906, he attended Bowun Academy in Wuchang. After graduating in 1912, he stayed on as an English teacher. In 1913, he entered Tsinghua School and the following year went to the United States. He studied at Wesleyan University in Connecticut, the Massachusetts Institute of Technology for civil engineering, and Columbia University for business, receiving his master‚Äôs degree in 1918. Upon returning to China in 1919, he taught at Nanjing Higher Normal School and Southeast University (the precursor of Central University).

After the death of Sun Yat-sen in 1925, the government decided to build the Sun Yat-sen Mausoleum in Nanjing. Tu collaborated with other engineers on constructing the tree-lined avenue leading to the mausoleum.

In 1927, he began teaching at Soochow University. In 1930, he received a Rockefeller Foundation scholarship and resumed studies in the United States at the University of Chicago, focusing on atomic physics. He earned a PhD in physics in 1932 and returned to continue teaching at Soochow University, eventually serving as the head of the physics department.

Liu Liangmo later played a key role in various national movements, including the national salvation movement. In 1939, he held the position of secretary for the Shanghai chapter of the YMCA and later worked to strengthen the organization‚Äôs outreach and community services during the war period.

In 1951, Tu Yuqing joined the Christian Youth Association, where he continued to be active in educational and religious activities. However, as the political landscape in China changed, he became involved in the three-self reform movement initiated by Wu Yaozong, becoming a significant figure in this transition. Despite facing considerable challenges during the Cultural Revolution, Tu remained an influential figure within the Chinese Christian community.

Finally, he passed away in September 1975 due to health complications after enduring years of political strife.

  1. Xie Fuya (Xie Fuya 1992; He Qingchang 2013)

Xie Fuya (N. Z. Xie, 1902-1992), originally from Zhejiang, was actively involved in the Christian movements during a tumultuous era in China. His experiences reflect the struggles and adaptations of the church under the changing political context of the time.Zia (Nai Zin Zia, 1892-1991) was born in Shaoxing, Zhejiang. His English name was derived from his time studying in Japan, where he was known as ‚ÄúNai Rin.‚Äù He received traditional education from a young age and went to Japan to study in 1911. In 1914, he enrolled in Tokyo Higher Normal School on a government scholarship but was expelled due to health issues. The following year, he transferred to Rikkyo University, where he became a Christian and was baptized at the Anglican Trinity Church in 1916. Due to ongoing health problems, he was unable to complete his studies and returned to China.

Starting in the fall of 1916, Zia worked in the publications department of the National Christian Council YMCA, and in 1918 he was transferred to the general affairs department as a clerk, serving as the Chinese assistant to General Secretary Yu Rizhzhang. Between 1925 and 1927, he was sponsored by the YMCA to study in the United States at the University of Chicago and Harvard University. After returning to China, he taught at Lingnan University and became active in Christian publishing, producing various works. In 1934, he resigned from his position at Lingnan to participate in the educational work of the People‚Äôs Education Promotion Association. He returned to Lingnan University in 1939, and in 1940 he became the dean of the College of Arts at Sun Yat-sen University. The following year, he served as director of the Civic Education Office at Hunan National Normal University. In 1943, he taught in the Chinese Literature Department at Soochow University. In 1945, he taught at the Guangxi Xijiang Institute. In 1946, he moved to Nanjing to teach at Jinling University, and in 1948, he returned to Hunan as the president of the National Normal University before eventually fleeing south to Lingnan University in Guangzhou due to the war.

In July 1949, Zia left Guangzhou for the British colony of Hong Kong to escape the turmoil. He described this as ‚Äúfleeing for his life‚Äù and stated that he ‚Äúhad to rush to an unwelcome foreign colony.‚Äù He later taught at Chung Chi College and Baptist College in Hong Kong and left for the United States in April 1958, facilitated by Francis P. Jones, a Methodist missionary who had previously taught at Jinling University in Nanjing. During his time in Hong Kong, Zia focused on the relationship between Christianity and Confucianism, contemplating the renewal of Chinese culture and ultimately promoting the construction of ‚ÄúChinese Christianity.‚Äù Simultaneously, he reflected on the sociopolitical issues in Hong Kong and Taiwan amidst his displacement.

Zia‚Äôs relocation to the United States in 1958 was organized by Jones, where he participated in the compilation of ‚ÄúA Collection of Christian Classics Through the Ages.‚Äù In 1984, he was invited to visit China by Sun Yat-sen University in Guangzhou and ultimately settled there in 1986, where he lived until his passing in 1991.

Christian or Pro-Christian Organizations

Chinese Christian Population (Ying 2009: 65, 75-78)

In 1949, the National Christian Council conducted a nationwide survey regarding the number of Chinese Christians. Although the data was incomplete, it remains of some authority. The survey was coordinated by Áï¢ÁØÑÂÆá (Frank W. Price), initially published in early October 1949, and then reissued in January 1950, both as mimeographed trial editions, with no differences between the two. In December 1950, the council published a revised letterpress edition. Comparing the two, the January edition reported a total of 792,862 church members (across 18 denominations), while the December edition reported 902,805 (across 21 denominations). At the same time, the council also compiled ‚ÄúA Brief Compilation of Chinese Christian Denominations 1949-1950‚Äù (manuscript), which included statistical data from various denominations. The ‚ÄúCompilation‚Äù recorded numbers for several denominations that were not noted in the ‚ÄúSurvey,‚Äù totaling 1,031,021 church members across these denominations. It can be said that the ‚ÄúSurvey‚Äù and the ‚ÄúCompilation‚Äù provided data on the number of Chinese Christians and their denominational distribution before and after the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China. However, due to the inconsistency of the reporting years from various denominations (for example, ÂÜÖÂú∞‰ºö provided data from 1934), and because the enumeration of denominations was not comprehensive, the actual number of Christians should exceed one million.

After the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China, the State Council required church groups to register. According to statistics from the Religious Affairs Bureau, in 1950 the total number of believers from 22 different Christian denominations across the country was 1,005,699. In fact, some denominations submitted numbers to the State Council that matched those in the ‚ÄúCompilation‚Äù and ‚ÄúSurvey,‚Äù but additionally included member counts from Christian meeting halls and spiritual service groups. A comparison shows that the member counts for most denominations were the same, reflecting that these denominations had only submitted their old records during registration. It is noteworthy that the records from the State Council included numbers for local sects such as Âü∫Áù£ÂæíËÅöÊúÉËôï, ÁúüËÄ∂Á©åÊïôÊúÉ, ËÄ∂Á©åÂÆ∂Â∫≠, and Ëá™Á´ãÊúÉ, which not only help us understand the situation of these local sects but also provide a more comprehensive grasp of the total number of Chinese Christians. In any case, it is generally regarded that the number of Christians in the early days of the new China is 700,000, a figure that warrants revision.Denominations

  1. Preparatory Committee of the Chinese Christian Anti-American Aid to Korea Three-Self Reform Movement 1951 (ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 1996: 55-62; ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 2012b: 58-64; ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 2023: 119-120)

After the Chinese Communist Party intervened in the Korean War, on December 16, 1950, the U.S. government announced the control of properties in territories it governed in China and North Korea, as well as prohibiting vessels from the U.S. from sailing to Chinese ports. In light of the U.S. freezing of Chinese public and private properties in the U.S., the Central People‚Äôs Government State Council also issued an order on December 28 to control American properties in China and freeze U.S. public and private deposits in China. This action severely impacted missionary work in China, which still relied on American funds for sustenance. On December 29, the State Council announced decisions on handling cultural, educational, and relief institutions, as well as religious groups receiving U.S. subsidies, which stated that ‚ÄúChinese religious groups accepting American subsidies should be transformed into organizations completely independent and self-managed by Chinese Christians. The government encourages their self-sustaining movements.‚Äù

From April 16 to 21, 1951, the Religious Affairs Bureau of the Ministry of Culture and Education of the Central People‚Äôs Government convened a ‚ÄúConference on Handling Christian Groups Accepting American Subsidies‚Äù in Beijing. The purpose of this government-convened meeting was to seek ‚Äúto finally, thoroughly, permanently, and completely end more than a century of cultural aggression by American imperialism against the Chinese people under the leadership of the Central People‚Äôs Government.‚ÄùThe conference was convened under the name of ‚ÄúConference on Handling Christian Groups Accepting American Subsidies,‚Äù but in essence, it was not merely addressing these ‚Äútechnical‚Äù issues. A comprehensive view of the meeting‚Äôs content reveals that the core topic was how to accelerate severing the ties between Christianity and imperialism and to initiate a ‚Äústruggle‚Äù between the church and American imperialism. As noted by Èô∏ÂÆö‰∏Ä (Lu Dingyi), deputy director of the Ministry of Culture and Education of the State Council, at the meeting: ‚ÄúHandling Christian groups that accept American subsidies is not a technical issue; it is a struggle. The goal of this struggle is to thoroughly eliminate the influence of over a hundred years of American imperialism‚Äôs cultural aggression against our country.‚Äù Moreover, American cultural aggression is not just a historical issue; it is a current reality that continues to manifest through Christianity.

As a result, over 150 church representatives from across the country, including many self-governing churches unrelated to Western denominations, such as Êï¨Â•†ÁÄõ (Jesus Family), ÂÄ™ÊüùËÅ≤ (Christian Meeting Place), and È≠è‰ª•Êíí (True Jesus Church), passed the ‚ÄúProcedures for Handling Christian Groups Accepting American Subsidies‚Äù and the ‚ÄúJoint Declaration of Representatives from Various Chinese Christian Churches and Organizations.‚Äù At the same time, to more comprehensively promote the Christian reform movement and lead Chinese churches to undertake the task of building a new China, the Beijing conference decided to establish a new organization‚Äîthe ‚ÄúPreparatory Committee for the Chinese Christian Anti-American Aid to Korea Three-Self Reform Movement,‚Äù specifically responsible for coordinating Christian reform efforts, with Âê≥ËÄÄÂÆó as the chairman of the preparatory committee. All Christian groups and denominations, including the council, were expected to undertake learning and reform under the guidance of the reform preparatory committee.

The establishment of the Three-Self Reform Preparatory Committee not only targeted reforms within Christianity but also reflected the CCP‚Äôs attempt to establish patriotic religious groups across major religions, thereby achieving unified leadership in the religious sphere. In the 1950s, various religions subsequently established patriotic religious groups supported by the party state: the ‰∏≠ÂõΩ‰ΩõÊïôÂçè‰ºö (Chinese Buddhist Association, 1953), the ‰∏≠ÂõΩ‰ºäÊñØÂÖ∞ÊïôÂçè‰ºö (Chinese Islamic Association, 1953), the ‰∏≠ÂõΩÈÅìÊïôÂçè‰ºö (Chinese Taoist Association, 1957), and the ‰∏≠ÂõΩÂ§©‰∏ªÊïôÊïôÂèãÁà±ÂõΩ‰ºö (Patriotic Catholic Association, 1957).

  1. ÈáëÈôµÂçîÂíåÁ•ûÂ≠∏Èô¢ (Jinling Union Theological Seminary) 1953 (ÂæêÂ¶ÇÈõ∑ 2022: 244-248; Ê±™Á∂≠Ëó© 2013: 1-2)

By the end of 1950, after China intervened in the Korean War, Sino-U.S. relations were completely broken, and the Chinese government announced a freeze on all U.S. assets in China. The economic aid that Christian missionary work had traditionally relied upon was thus interrupted, leading various seminaries to the brink of closure. In 1951, to reduce expenses, Jinling Theological Seminary merged with Jinling Women‚Äôs Theological Seminary, setting a precedent for institutional consolidation.

In August 1952, a theological education symposium was held in Shanghai in the East China region, where it was decided to establish a preparatory committee for the East China United Theological Seminary. In November, Jinling Union Theological Seminary was officially established in Nanjing, formed by the union of 11 seminaries in the East China region: Shanghai Anglican Central Theological Seminary, Shanghai Baptist Theological Seminary, Hangzhou Chinese Theological Seminary, Nanjing Jinling Theological Seminary (which included Jinling Women‚Äôs Theological Seminary), Wuxi North China Theological Seminary, Jinan Qilu Theological Seminary, Zhangzhou Minnan Theological Seminary, Fuzhou Union Theological Seminary, Ningbo Trinity Bible Institute, Zhenjiang Baptist Bible Institute, and Jinan Mingdao Bible Institute.

The 11 seminaries included both seminaries under church universities and those jointly run by denominations, as well as independent seminaries. In terms of theological positions, they encompassed both fundamentalist traditions and those leaning towards modernist theologians. At that time, in order to prevent faith from being influenced, representatives of the fundamentalist faction suggested merging into two seminaries‚Äîmodernists uniting at Nanjing Jinling Theological Seminary and fundamentalists at Wuxi North China Theological Seminary, leading to a stalemate in opinions at the conference. ÁΩóÁ´πÈ£é (Luo Zhufeng), head of the East China Religious Affairs Bureau, stated: ‚ÄúUnder the premise of anti-imperialism and patriotism, any theological thought has the freedom to exist, and the government will not interfere. Various theological thoughts in China cannot be considered to have a singular authority. In other words, since religious belief is free, our theological thoughts must also be free.‚Äù The principle of mutual respect at Jinling Union Theological Seminary emerged from this line of thinking. The school‚Äôs guiding principles were four ‚Äúcornerstones of faith‚Äù: ‚Äú1. The entire Bible is God‚Äôs revelation, including all essential doctrines concerning salvation, serving as the foundation for believers‚Äô faith and moral conduct. 2. The one God is the source of all things and the Father of humanity, full of justice and love. 3. Jesus Christ is the Holy Son of God, who became flesh to redeem mankind, was crucified, resurrected from the dead, and is the head of the church, the Savior of all ages. 4. The Holy Spirit is one of the Trinity, working in regeneration and sanctification, bestowing various gifts to believers in the church.‚Äù Note that all four points are listed as ‚Äú1,‚Äù without any order of precedence.

To accommodate different theological traditions, Jinling Union Theological Seminary adopted the principle of ‚Äúmutual respect‚Äù in its early years.Among the more than 30 faculty members at Jinling Union Theological Seminary, one-third came from a fundamentalist background, while over 60% of the more than 100 students adhered to fundamentalist traditions. Balancing the debates between ‚Äúmodernists‚Äù and ‚Äúfundamentalists‚Äù became a crucial issue that the seminary had to address. At that time, the Jinling Education Office stipulated that certain courses within theology and biblical studies be divided into classes based on either ‚Äúmodernist‚Äù or ‚Äúfundamentalist‚Äù viewpoints, with no directives for a ‚Äúunified faith.‚Äù

Between 1957 and 1959, the China Spiritual Seminary, Chongqing Theological Seminary, and Guangzhou Union Theological Seminary merged into Jinling Union Theological Seminary. The Chongqing Theological Seminary and China Spiritual Seminary were respectively administered by ÈôàÂ¥áÊ°Ç (Chen Chonggui) and Ë¥æÁéâÈì≠ (Jia Yuming), who had spiritual backgrounds. Their participation in the Three-Self Movement was believed to be based on considerations of unifying the spiritual faction, allowing them to operate independently. However, following the Anti-Rightist Movement, the spiritual faction was largely purged from the Three-Self organizations. Chen Chonggui was labeled a rightist, and Jia Yuming was also marked as part of the inner party, resulting in the merging of both spiritually inclined seminaries into Jinling Union Theological Seminary.

In 1961, Yenching Union Theological Seminary was further merged into Jinling, making Jinling the only theological seminary in the country, which resumed classes in April 1961. However, due to the severe impact of the Great Leap Forward on religious activities, Jinling could only offer continuing education programs for pastoral coworkers. By 1962, undergraduate courses were reinstated. Nevertheless, starting in 1962, the seminary was influenced by the ‚ÄúFour Clean-Ups‚Äù Movement (which aimed to clean politics, the economy, organizations, and thoughts), leading to a suspension of new student admissions. In 1966, with the outbreak of the Cultural Revolution, the last remaining seminary established a command center for the Red Guards, and the seminary was forced to close, plunging Chinese theological education into a dark period lasting over a decade.

  1. Yenching Union Theological Seminary 1953 (Âæê‰ª•È©ä 1999: 68-151)

In December 1950, the United States announced the freezing of all Chinese assets in America, and numerous educational, charitable, cultural, and religious organizations that relied heavily on American subsidies faced an immediate financial crisis. On January 15, 1951, the Ministry of Education held a meeting for higher education institutions receiving foreign subsidies, where Minister È©¨Âèô‰º¶ (Ma Xulun) pointed out that church schools were significant activities in American imperialism‚Äôs cultural aggression. In response to the U.S. freeze on Chinese assets, the State Council decided to take over educational sovereignty, converting the previously subsidized higher education institutions into schools run by the Chinese people themselves. After the meeting, the Ministry of Education of the Central People‚Äôs Government and the Education Department of the East China Military and Political Committee announced the takeover of Union Medical College and Yenching University. On February 12, Yenching University was officially taken over by the Ministry of Education.

With the freezing of U.S. funds, Yenching University suddenly lost all subsidies. As Yenching University was taken over by the government, the future of the religious school came under scrutiny. President ÈôÜÂøóÈü¶ (Lu Zhiwei) suggested reorganizing the religious school to become an academic research institution for various Chinese religions, thus allowing the government to permit it to function as a department within the university. However,Èô¢ÈïøËµµÁ¥´ÂÆ∏ (Zhao Zichen) preferred to separate the university from the religious school, stating that the only way forward after separation would be ‚Äúto let the church support itself.‚Äù To this end, Zhao convened a meeting of all faculty and students and invited some alumni in Beijing to participate in the discussion. A few days later, Zhao drafted a document titled ‚ÄúThe Future Direction of Yenching Religious Academy,‚Äù proposing to rename the religious academy as Yenching Religious Academy, register it with the Beijing municipal government, and request approval from the Ministry of Education to become a private religious academy.

After the government took over Yenching University, it immediately launched a patriotic education campaign, organizing seminars and denunciation meetings aimed at purging the harmful effects of American cultural aggression. Starting in July 1951, the religious academy at Yenching University also began learning research and engaged in criticism and self-criticism. After several months of study, the Beijing Religious Affairs Bureau planned to hold a meeting at the religious academy to denounce American imperialism‚Äôs use of religion as a means of aggression. The denunciation meeting was held on November 24, lasting the entire day, during which Zhao Zichen made accusations.

During the Three-Anti and Five-Anti Movements, the Beijing Municipal Committee of the CCP dispatched a ‚Äúwork team‚Äù to Yenching University in December 1951 to lead the movement. The president was sidelined, and classes were suspended to carry out the movement. Although the religious academy had already separated from the university, the discipline inspection committee of Yenching University still centrally directed the religious academy‚Äôs activities. In early March 1952, Zhao Zichen, as the head of the religious academy, was subjected to two rounds of accusations from the entire school, requiring him to conduct a thorough self-examination. Ultimately, Zhao was deemed to have a ‚Äústubborn‚Äù attitude and was removed from his position as president in March due to accusations from the ‚ÄúEconomy Inspection Committee,‚Äù including ‚Äúusing religion to organize and systematically spread toxins that undermine the new China; serving as one of the heads of reactionary religious groups worldwide; and, as a representative in the Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference, appearing to comply with government policies while actually opposing them.‚Äù As a result, he ‚Äúaccepted the demands of the masses‚Äù and had his position terminated, with ËíãÁøºÊåØ (Jiang Yizhen) serving as the acting head.In May 1952, the movement was declared concluded. In November, the religious academy moved away from Yanyuan. As early as May 1952, Yenching Religious Academy, Beijing Theological Seminary, and Union Women‚Äôs Theological College began preliminary discussions about merging the three institutions and negotiated unification in August. In April 1953, the three seminaries officially merged to form ‚ÄúYenching Union Theological Seminary,‚Äù with Áéã‰ª≤Ê¢ì (Wang Zhongzi) and ËíãÁøºÊåØ (Jiang Yizhen) serving as the chair and vice-chair of the administrative committee, respectively. In September, the Far East Missionary Alliance Holiness Bible College, Shenyang Northeast Theological Seminary, and Henan Kaifeng Huanei Baptist Theological Seminary also merged into Yenching. Between 1954 and 1956, Hubei Lankou Lutheran Theological Seminary, Hunan Bible College, Beijing Assembly of God Truth College, Central China Union Theological Seminary, and Beijing Xiangshan Spiritual Institute joined in succession.

In 1961, Yenching Union Theological Seminary was merged into Jinling Union Theological Seminary.

  1. China Christian Three-Self Patriotic Movement Committee 1954 (ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 2010: 111-116)

After the establishment of the Three-Self Reform Preparatory Committee in April 1951, nearly three years of efforts were still met with rejection from certain spiritual faction churches citing ‚Äúdifferent beliefs‚Äù and ‚Äúreforming faith.‚Äù The impact of churches such as Christian Meeting Place and the church of ÁéãÊòéÈÅì (Wang Mingdao) was particularly significant. Strengthening and expanding the unity among Chinese Christians under the overarching principle of ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform‚Äù became an important issue that needed to be addressed.

From November 27 to December 16, 1953, the Second National Religious Work Conference was held in Beijing. The conference was presided over by ‰π†‰ª≤Âãã (Xi Zhongxun), Minister of the Central Propaganda Department and Deputy Director of the Ministry of Culture and Education, and ÈôÜÂÆö‰∏Ä (Lu Dingyi), Deputy Director of the Ministry of Culture and Education, summarizing the Christian work from 1950 to 1953 and formulating future work guidelines. According to the meeting documents, to ‚Äúimplement the patriotic reform movement,‚Äù the future work‚Äôs focus would be ‚Äúto gain the broad support of devout believers while also winning over the majority of pastoral staff.‚Äù This way, it would be possible to ‚Äúexpand the China Christian Three-Self Reform Preparatory Committee as a leading organization by more comprehensively including representative upper leaders from the churches, making it a genuine patriotic united front organization for all Christian denominations in the country.‚Äù To achieve this goal, it was emphasized that the slogan should focus on ‚Äúpatriotism‚Äù and not emphasize the slogans of ‚ÄúThree-Self‚Äù or ‚Äúreform,‚Äù and that a formal organization should be established in the first half of 1954, possibly using the name ‚ÄúChristian Patriotic Association.‚Äù To unite more believers, patriotic reform organizations and active patriots must genuinely ‚Äúcare for the religious lives of believers,‚Äù as a fundamental means of connecting with the laity and a major condition for religious mass work.

From May 17 to 22, 1954, the Three-Self Reform Preparatory Committee held its second plenary meeting, attended by the heads of the Central Religious Affairs Bureau, ‰ΩïÊàêÊπò (He Chengxiang), ÁΩóÁ´πÈ£é (Luo Zhufeng), and ‰∏äÊµ∑ÂÆóÊïô‰∫ãÂä°Â§Ñ (Shanghai Religious Affairs Bureau) director Èôà‰∏ÄÈ∏£ (Chen Yiming). The meeting decided to convene a national Christian conference in Beijing in the second half of the year and to establish a ‚Äúformal leading organization.‚Äù Clearly, the national Christian conference was convened primarily under the directives of the Central Committee‚Äôs Christian work, with the goals of ‚Äúgreat unity‚Äù and the name of the new organization set in accordance with the CCP‚Äôs evaluation of the Christian reform movement. The party state was not satisfied with only individual denominational churches participating in the Three-Self Reform, and resolving the resistance from influential spiritual factions to the movement became a focal point of Christian work.

Prior to the 1954 National Christian Conference, the preparatory office extended invitations to representatives of the spiritual factions. The conference secretary general Ê≤àÂæ∑Ê∫∂ (Shen Derong) pointed out that to ‚Äúfurther expand the anti-imperialist patriotic unity of Chinese Christianity,‚Äù it was decided to invite ‚Äúrepresentatives from certain denominations and groups that have not participated or actively oppose the China Christian Three-Self Reform Movement.‚Äù In the end, 27 representatives from spiritual factions participated in the Three-Self Conference for the first time, with 15 from Shanghai alone. However, due to the boycott by ÁéãÊòéÈÅì and other spiritual factions, five invited representatives from Beijing declined to attend.

From July 22 to August 6, 1954, the China Christian National Conference was held in Beijing. Âê≥ËÄÄÂÆó (Wu Yaozong) suggested that for the sake of unity, the name of the formally established leading organization be changed from the previously used ‚ÄúChristian Three-Self Reform Movement‚Äù to ‚ÄúChristian Three-Self Patriotic Movement.‚Äù This was because the term ‚Äúreform‚Äù easily evoked associations with historical religious reform movements in the church. ‚ÄúSome comrades mistakenly believe that ‚Äòreform‚Äô means ‚Äòto abolish the church‚Äôs system‚Äô; others fear that ‚Äòreform‚Äô may interfere with their own beliefs, thus adopting a passive observation stance towards the Three-Self Reform Movement.‚Äù Therefore, the renaming of the ‚ÄúChristian Three-Self Patriotic Movement‚Äù would help eliminate factors that hinder church unity. He even emphasized that the Three-Self Patriotic Movement would become ‚Äúa movement for all Chinese believers, a great unity of all Chinese believers.‚Äù‚ÄúAny denomination, any church and believer with differing beliefs should work together on the basis of patriotism and love for their faith to contribute the greatest strength to the Three-Self Patriotic Movement.‚Äù During the National Christian Conference, ‰ΩïÊàêÊπò (He Chengxiang), Director of the Central Religious Affairs Bureau, criticized those who refused to participate in the patriotic movement citing ‚Äúdifferent beliefs,‚Äù but he still hoped that the spiritual factions could return to a common foundation of patriotism.

The chairman of the China Christian Three-Self Patriotic Movement Committee was Âê≥ËÄÄÂÆó (Wu Yaozong), with ÊùéÂÑ≤Êñá (Li Chuwen) serving as the secretary-general. The conference passed the ‚ÄúLetter to All National Colleagues,‚Äù reaffirming that ‚Äúwe understand that the purpose of self-governance is not to unify or alter the church‚Äôs systems, but to completely sever ties with imperialism, allowing Chinese Christians to unite and manage their own churches; self-supporting does not interfere with each church‚Äôs economy but aims for each church to entirely cut off its economic connections with imperialism and be fully responsible to Chinese believers; and self-propagating is not about unifying beliefs or altering faith, but about thoroughly purging the remnants of imperialist ideological influence so that each church preaches the pure gospel consistent with the Bible. As for the differences in faith, systems, and rituals among churches, we should mutually respect one another.‚Äù This can be seen as a response to the spiritual factions.

  1. Christian Joint Publishing Bureau 1956 (ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 2012b: 228-232, 239-260)

According to a survey by the China Christian Council in 1950, the national Christian publishing institutions included: ‰∏≠ËèØ‰ø°Áæ©Êõ∏Â†±ÈÉ® (Chinese Lutheran Press), ‰∏≠ËèØÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÊõ∏Â±Ä (Chinese Baptist Publishing House), ‰∏≠ËèØÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÂá∫ÁâàÂçîÊúÉ (Chinese Christian Publishing Association), ‰∏≠ÂúãÂü∫Áù£ÊïôËÅñÊïôÊõ∏ÊúÉ (Chinese Christian Sacred Book Society), Â§©È¢®Á§æ (Heavenly Wind Society), Áî∞ÂÆ∂ÂçäÊúàÂàä (Tianjia Biweekly), ÈùíÂπ¥ÂçîÊúÉÊõ∏Â±Ä (Youth Association Publishing House), ÂÆ£ÈÅìÊõ∏Â±Ä (Evangelical Publishing House), ÊôÇÂÖÜÂ†±È§® (Shizhao Press), ËèØËã±Êõ∏Â±Ä (Huaying Publishing House), Âª£ÂçîÊõ∏Â±Ä (Guangxie Publishing House), Âª£Â≠∏ÊúÉ (Guangxue Society), Èùà‰øÆÊó•Á®ãÁ§æ (Spiritual Calendar Society), Èñ©ÂåóÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÊúÉÊõ∏Â±Ä (Northern Min Christian Church Publishing House), ‰∏≠ËèØËÅñÂÖ¨ÊúÉ‰∏≠Â§ÆËæ¶‰∫ãËôïÊõ∏ÂÆ§ (Central Office of the Chinese Anglican Church Publishing House), ‰∏äÊµ∑Á¶èÈü≥Âª£Êí≠ÈõªÂè∞ (Shanghai Gospel Radio Station), and others totaling 17 institutions.

In January 1951, the East China Military and Political Committee required all Christian publishing establishments receiving foreign subsidies to register with relevant authorities. In the new context of the Korean War, the Christian publishing industry faced severe challenges. On March 16, a Christian publishing conference was held in Beijing to assist the Christian publishing sector in overcoming the difficulties arising from the cessation of U.S. funds after the outbreak of the Korean War, and to discuss how to sever ties with imperialism and eliminate its influence. The conference decided that the Christian Publishing Association would be responsible for book reviews, providing feedback to various publishing units to remove the influence of imperialism. Additionally, it proposed a unified operation of the Christian publishing sector, aiming to establish a joint distribution entity.

After the publishing conference, the association pushed for the establishment of Christian joint bookstores. Between March and June 1951, the association convened two discussions with Christian publishing institutions in Shanghai and consulted various organizations across the nation. It is noteworthy that the responses were ‚Äúprincipally supportive, though there were some considerations regarding actual participation conditions,‚Äù reflecting some resistance from existing publishing institutions toward the unification proposal. The earliest supporters were only Guangxue Society, Youth Association Publishing House, and Baptist Bookstore, which sent representatives (Âê¥ËÄÄÂÆó, Âº†‰ªïÁ´†, ËÉ°Á•ñËî≠, Âº†‰ªÅÈïú, ÈáëÁÇéÈùí, and Âº†‰ºØÊÄÄ) to form a preparatory committee, deciding to first establish a central joint entity in Shanghai. However, further achieving the merger of the various Christian publishing institutions involved more complex issues, with the willingness of independent publishing entities to yield and submit to the leadership of the Christian Publishing Association being a primary concern.

Between 1952 and 1953, fewer than 10 publishing entities continued to operate, collectively publishing 44 Christian works, reflecting serious setbacks in the Christian publishing industry under political pressure. In 1953, the Christian Publishing Association ended itself due to a lack of necessity for continuation. Four entities, including the Guangxue Society, Chinese Baptist Publishing House, Youth Association National Council Publishing Department, and China Sunday School Alliance, formed a united editorial committee in 1953 according to the spirit of the Christian publishing conference as the first step towards unification. The united editorial committee consisted of the main editorial staff from the four entities, with Ê≤àÂæ∑Ê∫∂ (Shen Derong) serving as executive secretary to coordinate the publication plans, while the actual publishing responsibilities remained with the respective entities. This indicates that the Three-Self Reform organization had begun to manage and coordinate the reform plans for Christian publishing. In 1954 and 1955, the Guangxue Society, Chinese Baptist Publishing House, Youth Association Publishing House, and China Sunday School Alliance collaborated on editorial work and published three titles under the Guangxue Society, including two volumes of ‚ÄúNew Sermon Collections‚Äù and ‚ÄúThe Life and Teachings of Prophet Jeremiah‚Äù (by ÊùéÊ¶ÆËä≥). Meanwhile, various publishing houses still published over 30 works under their independent names.In December 1956, the Christianity Joint Publishing Bureau was formed through the alliance of the Guangxue Society, Chinese Baptist Publishing House, China Sunday School Alliance, and Youth Association Publishing House, with Ê±üÊñáÊº¢ (Jiang Wenhan) serving as the general secretary. The establishment of the Christianity Joint Publishing Bureau marked the formal entry of the Christian publishing industry into an era of ‚Äúunification.‚Äù After its establishment, the Joint Publishing Bureau published a total of 13 books in 1957. However, following the Anti-Rightist Movement, the work of the Christianity Joint Publishing Bureau faced unprecedented challenges. By the 1960s, there were no more advertisements for new book publications in the magazine Â§©È¢® (Tianfeng), reflecting a halt in the publishing activities of the Joint Publishing Bureau. During the Cultural Revolution, with the cessation of the Three-Self organizations, the Joint Publishing Bureau also faced a similar fate of closure.

  1. China Christian Shanghai Spiritual Theological Seminary (Â§©È¢® 1956a: 28, 1956b: 3; ÁéãÂæ∑Èæç 2017: 146-164)

After the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China, the central government began to address the system of theological education in China, primarily through unification efforts. Utilizing the funding disruption post-Korean War, several seminaries were merged into three ‚Äúunified‚Äù seminaries: Jinling Union Theological Seminary (Nanjing), Yenching Union Theological Seminary (Beijing), and Guangzhou Union Theological Seminary (Guangzhou). By 1956, these three unifying seminaries had merged over 30 seminaries from various regions of the country.

However, outside of these three ‚Äúunified‚Äù seminaries, there were still some individual seminaries that managed to persist. The first category was seminaries run by spiritual factions that joined the Three-Self Movement: besides the influential ÈáçÂ∫ÜÁ•ûÂ≠¶Èô¢ (Chongqing Theological Seminary) led by ÈôàÂ¥áÊ°Ç (Chen Chonggui) and Ë¥æÁéâÈì≠ (Jia Yuming), there was also the Huai‚Äôen Amateur Theological Seminary established by ÊàöÂ∫ÜÊâç (Qi Qingcai) at the Baptist Church. The second category consisted of independent seminaries run by individuals from the spiritual faction. Due to reservations regarding the Three-Self Movement, these seminaries continued to operate independently. Though smaller in scale and unconnected to larger organizations, they relied mainly on faith-based contributions, enabling them to survive even after the ‚Äúliberation.‚Äù Examples include the Âåó‰∫¨È¶ôÂ±±ÁÅµ‰øÆÈô¢ (Beijing Xiangshan Spiritual Institute) led by ÊØïÂíèÁê¥ (Bi Yongqin), the ‰∏≠ÂõΩ‰º†ÈÅì‰∫∫‰øÆÂÖªÈô¢ (China Christian Worker‚Äôs Home, led by ÁÑ¶Áª¥Áúü), and the Âú£ÁªèÂ≠¶Á§æ (Bible Society) in Shanghai.

During the 1954 National Christian Conference, the China Christian Three-Self Patriotic Movement Committee was established, aimed at encouraging the participation of the spiritual faction in the Three-Self Movement. ÁÑ¶Áª¥Áúü attended the conference, while ÊØïÂíèÁê¥ refused to do so. However, after the arrest of the anti-Three-Self activist ÁéãÊòéÈÅì in 1955, many within the spiritual faction, including ÊØïÂíèÁê¥, decided to join the Three-Self Movement, likely with the intention of fostering unity among spiritual factions. The China Christian Worker‚Äôs Home, Huai‚Äôen Amateur Theological Seminary, and Bible Society did not merge into Jinling or Yenching Union Theological Seminaries but instead cooperated with Ë¥æÁéâÈì≠‚Äôs China Christian Spiritual Theological Seminary to form the ‰∏≠ÂõΩÂü∫Áù£Êïô‰∏äÊµ∑ÁÅµ‰øÆÁ•ûÂ≠¶Èô¢ (China Christian Shanghai Spiritual Theological Seminary), with Ë¥æÁéâÈì≠ as its president, and ÁÑ¶Áª¥Áúü, ÊàöÂ∫ÜÊâç, and Êù®ÁªçÂîê (Yang Shaotang) as vice presidents, and Á´∫ËßÑË∫´ (Zhu Guishen) as the chairman of the board. The seminary aimed to emphasize ‚Äúthis collaboration is centered on Christ, pooling the strength of each body to cultivate holy workers who love their country and church in the new China.‚Äù Its educational policy prioritized ‚Äúspiritual life and consistent biblical knowledge, aiming for students to achieve a correct balance in faith and life, knowledge and experience, so they may be useful to the Lord in building the church in the new China.‚Äù

In October 1956, the seminary held a founding ceremony where Ë¥æÁéâÈì≠ stated: ‚Äú‚ÄòChina Christianity‚Äô is our target audience, ‚ÄòShanghai‚Äô is the location of the seminary, ‚ÄòSpirituality‚Äô indicates our focus on experiential faith and abundant life, while ‚ÄòTheological Seminary‚Äô signifies our dedication to the study of biblical truth and theological knowledge.‚Äù The seminary hoped to concentrate its efforts on cultivating ‚Äúholy workers‚Äù who met the needs of the times.

However, following the onset of the Anti-Rightist Movement, Ë¥æÁéâÈì≠, ÁÑ¶Áª¥Áúü, ÊàöÂ∫ÜÊâç, and Êù®ÁªçÂîê were all required to go to Beijing for re-education. Both Ë¥æ and Êù® were classified as designated rightists, and all classes were suspended, compelling students and faculty to participate in educational activities in Shanghai and engage in labor to combat drought in Jiading County. In 1958, the Religious Affairs Bureau required that Three-Self churches were not to hire students from the Spiritual Academy as ministers, rescinding previously issued appointment letters due to perceived ideological issues, mandating further participation in educational activities. This re-education process continued until March 1959, at which point students from other provinces were sent back to their hometowns. The China Christian Shanghai Spiritual Theological Seminary was ordered closed, marking the end of the last seminary managed through spiritual faction cooperation, ultimately succumbing to the tides of revolution.

Christian or Pro-Christian Events/Movements

  1. National Christian Conference and Promotion Association 14th Annual Meeting 1950 (Song Jun 2017: 117-159, 213-248; Xing Fuzeng 2012b: 25-29, 43-47)

At the end of January 1950, the Executive Committee of the China National Christian Council decided to hold a national Christian conference in Beijing at Yenching University from August 19 to 27 under the theme ‚ÄúChristianity and the New Era.‚Äù A special preparatory committee was formed to follow up on related work. The committee was chaired by Zhu Youyu (Bishop of the China Anglican Church), with Tu Yuqing (Secretary-General of the National Fellowship), Bao Zheqing (Secretary-General of the Zhejiang Shanghai Baptist Association), Cui Xianxiang (Secretary-General of the National Council of the China Christian Church), and Jiang Changchuan (Bishop of the North China Conference of the China Methodist Church) as vice-chairpersons. Wu Gaozi (Secretary-General of the Promotion Association) was appointed as Secretary-General. Each member was a leader of major denominations and organizations nationwide. The preparatory committee decided to form four groups: the conference declaration, the ministry program, the reform plan, and cooperative organizations. On March 28, the national conference preparatory committee decided to postpone the full meeting. In July, Secretary-General Wu Gaozi proposed his resignation during an executive meeting, which was accepted by the Standing Committee of the Promotion Association. In August, since the national conference was too close to the 14th annual meeting of the Association, to streamline and save resources, it was decided to merge the conference into the annual meeting.

The postponement and eventual cancellation of the national conference reflected the difficulties it faced. On one hand, there were obviously differing opinions within the Chinese church regarding how to reform. The Promotion Association intended to play the role of representing Christianity to push for reform (the Promotion faction), which clearly diverged from the external progressive forces represented by ‚ÄúTianfeng‚Äù (the Tianfeng faction). On the other hand, more importantly, the Chinese Communist Party was unwilling to see the national conference proceed smoothly. It was clear that the party-state believed the reform work for Christianity should be led by its trusted ally‚ÄîWu Yaozong. If the national conference reached any consensus on church reform, it would undoubtedly become a major obstacle to Wu Yaozong‚Äôs reform efforts. While the Promotion Association was preparing for the national conference, Wu Yaozong, with the support of the party-state, launched a signature campaign for the reform declaration. This grand signature campaign and the Promotion Association seemingly became two different paths for the direction of reform in Chinese Christianity.

The competition for leadership in church reform between Wu Yaozong and the Promotion Association finally concluded with the convening of the 14th annual meeting of the Promotion Association in October 1950. To counter Wu Yaozong‚Äôs reform declaration, Vice Secretary-General Hayward (Victor E. W. Hayward) requested that the annual meeting issue a ‚ÄúLetter to National Believers,‚Äù discussing the Three-Self reform from a religious standpoint. Wu Yaozong was also excluded from the list of the declaration committee. To ensure that the direction of the annual meeting aligned with the vision of the Promotion Association, the Association hoped to retain the attendance rights of missionaries according to the original charter. Additionally, Hayward served as the executive secretary of the procedural committee for the annual meeting and discussed materials on the ‚ÄúChurch Reform Issue‚Äù of the national conference within the procedures to replace Wu Yaozong‚Äôs reform declaration.
