On October 9, the East China Military and Political Committee held a symposium, with Christian representatives invited, including: Wu Yaozong, Miao Qiusheng, Jiang Changchuan, Wu Gaozi, Tu Yuqing, Jiang Wenhan, Ai Nian San, Liu Liangmo, Chen Jianzhen, Shao Jingsan, Zheng Jianye, and others. Government representatives included Shu Tong (Minister of the Propaganda Department of the East China Military and Political Committee), Pan Hanyan (Deputy Mayor of Shanghai, Minister of the United Front Department of East China Bureau), Zhou Erfu (Secretary-General of the United Front Department of East China Bureau), Mei Dajun (Cadre of the United Front Department), and Zhou Lixing (Director of the East China Religious Affairs Office). The party and government representative lineup was not light, indicating their authoritative presence. The symposium ultimately made two regulations for the annual meeting: first, foreign members of the Promotion Association‚Äôs Executive Committee should not attend the meeting; second, the annual meeting should uphold the objectives of the Christian reform declaration.

Under the active intervention of the CCP, the 14th annual meeting of the Promotion Association, held from October 18 to 25, ultimately passed a resolution supporting the declaration ‚ÄúThe Path for Chinese Christianity to Strive in the Construction of the New China‚Äù and called on believers nationwide to actively participate in the signature campaign. Additionally, the annual meeting decided to ‚Äúwith utmost determination, complete the Three-Self Movement within five years.‚Äù Wu Yaozong was also elected as Vice-President of the National Promotion Association amid the opposing forces of support and opposition. After the meeting, major newspapers in Shanghai reported the annual meeting‚Äôs news under titles such as ‚ÄúSevering the Ties Between Christianity and Imperialism.‚Äù(2) Three-Self Reform Declaration Signature Campaign 1950 (Xing Fuzeng 2012b: 25-52; Song Jun 2017: 161-211; Wickeri 1990: 127-133; Bush 1970: 177-187; MacInnis 1972: 158-160)

The brewing of the Three-Self Reform Declaration began with the results of Christian leaders‚Äô discussions with Zhou Enlai during their visit to Beijing. The formation of the Christian delegation was related to the preparations for the national Christian conference. It can be said that both were responses from the Chinese church reflecting on their future and reforms after the establishment of the New China, and they also mirrored the twists and turns of the two factions within the church (the Promotion faction and the Tianfeng faction) moving from unity to division.

In October 1949, the Promotion Association held its first Executive Committee meeting after the establishment of New China. It decided to convene a national representative conference of Christianity as soon as possible, to connect various denominations across the country, and to establish a national administrative body. It also decided to organize a delegation to connect with churches nationwide to facilitate the organization of the national conference. At the meeting, Wu Yaozong hoped to convey the significance and resolutions of the Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference to various regions. Thus, the Promotion Association, the National Association of Young Men and Women Christians of China, and the Christian representatives of the People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference jointly initiated the organization of the delegation.

In April 1950, the North China delegation was announced, consisting of Wu Yaozong, Liu Liangmo (Director of the Youth Association National Committee), Wu Gaozi, Tu Yuqing, Cui Xianxiang, Wang Zizhong (Secretary-General of the North China Christian United Association), Deng Yuzhi (Secretary-General of the National Women‚Äôs Youth Association), and others. After visiting Jinan from April 15 to 18, the delegation moved to Beijing. On the 30th, Wu, Liu, Deng, Tu, Cui, Wang, along with Zhao Zichan (Yenching School of Religion) and Zhao Fusang (Deputy Secretary-General of the Beijing Youth Association) held discussions with Deputy Minister Xu Bing of the Central United Front Department on religious issues, during which Wu Yaozong hoped to meet with Zhou Enlai.

On May 2, Zhou Enlai met with Wu Yaozong, Liu Liangmo, Deng Yuzhi, Cui Xianxiang, Wang Zizhong, Tu Yuqing, and Jiang Changchuan among others at the State Council. Chen Qiyuan, Deputy Minister of the Ministry of the Interior of the Central People‚Äôs Government, was also present. On May 6, Wu Yaozong and others returned to the State Council for a second discussion. Present were additional attendees including Ai Nian San (Shanghai Chinese Lutheran Church), Yang Xiaopeng (Secretary-General of the Tianjin Christian Youth Association), Ling Yuxiu (Chairperson of the Beijing Women‚Äôs Youth Association), Ling Xianyang (Beijing Diocese of the China Anglican Church), Zhao Fusang, and more. Chen Qiyuan and over ten officials from related departments were also in attendance, with discussions lasting more than three hours. At 11 PM on the 13th, Zhou held a third meeting with Wu Yaozong and 19 others, increasing the attendees to include Zhao Zichan, Lu Zhiwei (President of Yenching University), Jiang Changchuan, Gao Fengshan (Principal of Beijing Huiwen Middle School), Pang Huiting (also known as Pang Zhikun, Beijing Congregational Church), Chen Wenrun (Secretary-General of the Beijing Women‚Äôs Youth Association), Liu Weicheng (Shanghai Christian Mutual Association), Huo Peixiu (Tianjin Congregational Church), and Zheng Xisan (Chairman of the Tianjin Wesleyan Church and North China Conference). This discussion lasted four and a half hours and concluded at 3:30 AM the following day.

Christian leaders harbored great expectations for meeting with Zhou Enlai. At that time, the most pressing issues for them were the implementation of policies regarding religious freedom and corresponding measures for managing religion. They hoped the central government would issue directives for local people‚Äôs governments to strictly enforce the provisions on religious freedom outlined in the common program, to help resolve the current issues facing Christianity. However, Zhou pointed out that the main obstacle facing Christianity in China at present was its relationship with imperialism. The government required religious groups to clear and sever ties with imperialism and to establish self-governing, self-supporting, and self-propagating churches.

At Zhou Enlai‚Äôs request, Wu Yaozong later discussed revising the originally prepared ‚ÄúPreliminary Opinions on Handling Christian Issues,‚Äù which was subsequently renamed ‚ÄúThe Path for Chinese Christianity‚Äôs Future Efforts,‚Äù and submitted this revised version to Zhou Enlai after printing. Notably, Wu Gaozi, the official representative of the North China delegation, was not included on the list of Christian leaders Zhou met with three times. Wu later expressed that this was Zhou‚Äôs deliberate exclusion of him from the list. At that time, Wu held the positions of Secretary-General of the Promotion Association and Secretary-General of the National Conference Preparatory Committee; Zhou‚Äôs disregard for Wu Gaozi clearly indicated the CCP‚Äôs favoritism towards Wu Yaozong and a belittlement of the Promotion Association, aiming to undermine the preparations for the national conference.

On May 15, Wu, Liu, Tu, and Ai left Beijing and continued to visit Xi‚Äôan. On the train, Wu Yaozong revised ‚ÄúThe Path for Chinese Christianity‚Äôs Future Efforts‚Äù and sent the revised draft back to Beijing. On the 20th, the relevant departments of the Central Committee and the State Council held a discussion to review the fifth revised draft of the declaration. While in Xi‚Äôan, Wu Yaozong received Zhou Enlai‚Äôs response to the fifth revision and began to invite dozens of fellow believers within the country to co-sign it.Wu Yaozong, in order to garner more church leaders as co-signers and act as initiator, made concessions on certain opinions. For instance, he accepted the suggestions from Shanghai Christian leaders and modified the statement ‚ÄúChristian organizations should adhere to the principle of not using foreign personnel‚Äù to ‚ÄúChinese Christian churches and organizations that still rely on foreign talent and economic assistance should formulate concrete plans to achieve the goal of self-sufficiency in the shortest possible time.‚Äù Later, he removed the word ‚Äúimmediately‚Äù to avoid misunderstanding that foreign economic relations needed to be severed at once. Furthermore, he deleted the references in the preface of the fifth revision regarding the discussions with Premier Zhou, replacing them with an introduction that emphasized the significance of the declaration itself. Additionally, Beijing church leaders changed the wording in the introduction from ‚Äúthe countries that brought Christianity to China are mainly these imperialist nations‚Äù to ‚Äúthe people who brought Christianity to China are mainly from these imperialist countries.‚Äù Wu had expressed to the Shanghai church leaders that all modifications would need to be approved by Zhou Enlai. However, regarding the fundamental viewpoint on the relationship between Christianity and imperialism, Wu Yaozong remained uncompromising. The basic stance against imperialism provoked significant resentment among church members. On June 6, the Youth Association held a discussion on the declaration, which met with intense opposition from participants. On July 26, over 20 administrative staff from the Promotion Association requested Wu to remove the clauses in the declaration that criticized imperialism.

The ‚ÄúReform Declaration‚Äù includes four sections: the preface, general tasks, basic policies, and specific measures. The preface primarily acknowledges that Christianity, over its hundred-plus years of history in China, has ‚Äúestablished relationships with imperialism, both intentionally and unintentionally, in both tangible and intangible ways.‚Äù Even after the establishment of the New China, imperialism still attempted to use Christianity for subversion. The total mission of the Chinese church moving forward is to thoroughly uphold the common program, oppose imperialism, feudalism, and bureaucratic capitalism under government leadership, and strive for the construction of the new China. The basic policies include two aspects: first, the Chinese church must acknowledge the fact of being used by imperialism in the past and eliminate the ‚Äúimperialistic influence within Christianity‚Äù; second, it aims to achieve self-governance, self-support, and self-propagation as its reform goals in the short term. As for specific measures, concrete plans must be developed to explore how to achieve self-renewal in a short time and to improve the church in terms of religious and general operations.

On July 28, Wu Yaozong finalized the last (eighth revision) draft titled ‚ÄúThe Path for Chinese Christianity to Strive in the Construction of the New China,‚Äù along with the support of 40 Christian initiators, and submitted it for publication, also writing to over a thousand Christian leaders nationwide to solicit their individual signatures in support. Initially, Wu only planned to publish the declaration without intending to turn it into a wide-ranging signature campaign. In June, Zhou Enlai instructed that the declaration be transformed into a signature movement, aiming to leverage mass mobilization to pressure church leadership to accept the reform direction desired by the CCP and to establish Wu‚Äôs leadership position within the Christian reform movement.

The first round of signatures was concluded at the end of August 1950, with a total of 1,527 names received. On September 23, the People‚Äôs Daily published the full text of the declaration on the front page, including the complete list of the first batch of signatures. An editorial titled ‚ÄúThe Patriotic Movement of Christian Leaders‚Äù was also published to generate momentum for the declaration. The editorial affirmed the patriotic movement of Christian leaders and highly praised the signature campaign.

By the end of September 1950, ‚ÄúTianfeng‚Äù announced a second batch of names, increasing the total number of signatories to 3,268, adding 1,741 more than the first batch. Following the People‚Äôs Daily‚Äôs high-profile support for the declaration, various gatherings and symposiums were held nationwide in support of the Three-Self Movement and the Reform Declaration. By early December, the number of signatories had risen to 26,727, and by the end of December, it reached 78,596. By the end of January 1951, nearly 90,000 individuals had signed the Reform Declaration. The involvement and support of political forces played a significant role in the development of the signature campaign for the Reform Declaration. By April 1951, the number of signatures had increased to 200,000. By September 1952, the total number of signatures reached 338,000.

  1. Christian Publishing Review 1951 (Xing Fuzeng 2012b: 229-235)

On March 16, 1951, a Christian publishing conference was held in Beijing, aimed at assisting the Christian publishing industry in overcoming difficulties arising from the cessation of American funding after the outbreak of the Korean War, and to discuss how the publishing industry could sever ties with imperialism and eliminate its influences. The timing of the conference coincided with the nationwide anti-American and anti-imperialist movement, making the elimination of imperialist toxins a key focus of the party-state‚Äôs propaganda efforts.

In order to purge the ‚Äútoxic‚Äù elements from Christian publications, the review of Christian books and periodicals was comprehensively initiated after the conference.At that time, the China Christian Publishing Association listed ten standards for the review of publications: (1) authors recognized as traitors to Christianity; (2) direct or indirect, explicitly or implicitly anti-communist, anti-Soviet, or disparaging remarks about the People‚Äôs Government and its leaders; (3) containing imperialist, capitalist, or feudal thoughts; (4) emphasizing unrealistic ‚Äúsuper-political‚Äù ideas that are harmful to patriots; (5) propaganda for imperialism or agents of imperialism; (6) distorting or misunderstanding facts, insulting Chinese culture, and damaging the self-esteem and confidence of the Chinese people; (7) containing inflammatory or divisive ideas that hinder unity between the Chinese people and the government; (8) obstructing the anti-American aid to Korea efforts; (9) indiscriminately endorsing the ‚Äúuniversal church‚Äù manipulated by imperialism; (10) opposing or undermining the Three-Self Movement.

Under these standards, a large-scale political review of Christian publications was initiated. In May 1951, the publishing association required various publishing institutions to notify the association of their review results by the end of July. At the same time, it called on over 200 church organizations in Shanghai to review their collections and report back to the association. In July, the publishing association received review reports from 17 publishing institutions: Guangxuehui, Youth Association Bookstore, Baptist Bookstore, Tianfeng Society, Shizhao Press, China Bible Society, Chinese Sunday School Association, China Anglican Church Publications Committee, Christian Missionary Society, Guangxie Bookstore, Christian Gospel Bookstore, Salvation Bookstore, Gospel Publications, and the Christian Student Union of various universities (14 of these were in Shanghai), along with the China Christian Bible Society (Hankou) and the Literature Department of the Sichuan Conference of the China Christian Church (formerly the British Baptist Bookstore) and the Monthly on Youth Issues (both located in Chengdu). Among the 649 titles submitted for review by Guangxuehui, Youth Association Bookstore, and the Chinese Baptist Bookstore, 352 were deemed problematic, accounting for 54.2% of the total. These works containing ‚Äútoxic‚Äù elements were either removed from sale or edited.

In June 1951, at a mass protest meeting of the Christian community held in Shanghai, Wu Yaozong, Chairman of the Christian Three-Self Reform Preparatory Committee, pointed out that American imperialism used Christian publishing as ‚Äúan important tool for cultural invasion.‚Äù In addition to Guangxuehui and other publishing bodies producing numerous reactionary publications that spread toxins, the Bible also became a tool to narcotize the people and implant enslaving thoughts. At the same protest meeting, Hu Zuyin, the volunteer general secretary of Guangxuehui, also made accusations, claiming that ‚Äúthe imperialists‚Äô motive for founding Guangxuehui was not to promote the gospel of Jesus but to narcotize the thoughts of the Chinese people and eliminate their resistance to imperialism.‚Äù He critiqued from the perspective of ‚Äúcultural invasion,‚Äù admitting that among nearly 2,000 books published by Guangxuehui, at least 60% of the materials were translations from American publications, becoming a channel for spreading pro-American, admiration of America, and fear of America ideologies.

In August 1951, the China Christian Publishing Association, Guangxuehui, and the China Bible Society jointly held a conference in Shanghai to expose the crimes of American imperialism exploiting the Christian publishing industry to invade China. At the meeting, Zhang Bohuai, the general secretary of the Publishing Association, pointed out that ‚Äúmany Christian publishing organizations are almost entirely controlled by imperialist agents,‚Äù and their publications disseminate imperialist toxins. ‚ÄúWhether they are conservatives or reformists, spiritualists or social gospel advocates,‚Äù they are all being utilized by imperialists. This protest meeting targeted not only the various toxins found in printed materials but also pointed the finger at the Bible itself, accusing it of being a tool of imperialist cultural invasion. Miao Qiusheng from the China Bible Society indicated that spiritualist missionaries misinterpret the Bible, spreading toxic ideas of super-politics, super-nationalism, super-globalism, and the notion of ‚Äúloving one‚Äôs enemies,‚Äù thus facilitating imperialist invasion. At the meeting, Xie Zonggao and Liu Meili from Guangxuehui also conducted a comprehensive self-critique.

In September 1951, ‚ÄúTianfeng‚Äù published an editorial titled ‚ÄúPurging American Imperialist Influences in the Christian Publishing Industry,‚Äù stating that for many years, American imperialists, under the guise of ‚Äúliterary preaching,‚Äù published a large number of books and periodicals containing various ideological toxins. Both the review of publications and the accusations against publishing institutions were an integral part of the overall CCP‚Äôs efforts regarding Christian reform work. This scrutiny of Christian publications and the campaign of accusations undoubtedly represented a significant event in the history of Christian publishing in China, leading to a heavy blow to the literary publishing industry due to increasing politicization.

  1. First Cadre Training Class for the Christian Three-Self Reform Movement (Xing Fuzeng 1996: 77-80; 2012: 144-145)

In November 1951, the Preparatory Committee established the ‚ÄúFirst Cadre Training Class for the Christian Three-Self Reform Movement‚Äù in Shanghai, with the aim of training a group of cadre, especially active young individuals, to serve as the backbone for deeply promoting the Three-Self reforms.Similar training classes were also established in various locations. The Three-Self Reform Training Class lasted for three months, with 99 participants from different denominational churches and organizations in Shanghai. In addition to the members of the Three-Self Reform Preparatory Committee, the East China and Shanghai Religious Affairs Offices also sent nine colleagues, including Section Chief Yao Piaoting and Director Luo Zhufeng, to assist in the training.

The training class preparatory committee was chaired by Tu Yuqing, with Deng Yuzhi as vice-chair and Huang Peiyong as the secretary. Other committee members included Liu Liangmo, Li Chuwen, Situ Tong, Shi Ruzhang, and Shen Derong. The leading roles in the training class were clearly dominated by the Youth Association members, further demonstrating the significant role of the Youth Association faction.

The training program consisted of five main procedures: (1) clarifying learning objectives and establishing a correct learning attitude; (2) recognizing the greatness and love of the motherland: comparing old and new China, understanding the great achievements and future of the New China, and recognizing how the New China came to be; (3) understanding the nature of imperialism and its exploitation of Christianity for invasion: comprehending the essence of imperialism, understanding American imperialism‚Äôs aggression against China (including cultural invasion), and recognizing how imperialism uses Christianity for invasion; (4) understanding the significance of the Three-Self Reform Movement and the government‚Äôs religious policies; (5) learning summary: writing personal learning summaries, group exchanges, and group summaries. From the training class procedures, it was evident that participants were required to learn to support the New China in an anti-imperialist and patriotic manner; as believers or church workers, they were expected to uphold the Three-Self Reform Movement as a foundation.

One participant, Shen Yifan, son of Bishop Shen Zigao of the China Anglican Church, criticized his own thinking in his personal learning summary: ‚ÄúI had long believed that my benefits came from imperialism. In church schools, from elementary to university to seminary, I seemed to sail smoothly; thus, I hoped to study abroad in the UK and the US to obtain a degree and seek a high position within the church after returning. The liberation of China shattered all my personal plans, hence I did not love the New China. At the beginning of the liberation, I held a strong desire for a regime change. When the Korean War broke out, I even felt pleased, believing that the ‚Äòchange of regime‚Äô was imminent. Through a few years of realistic education, especially during this training, I understood the essence of our motherland, recognized the great achievements and bright future of the country; comparing my happy life today with the past, I emotionally affirmed that my fate is inseparable from that of the New China. I must stand from the people‚Äôs perspective to benefit myself.‚Äù

On December 11, the training class held a graduation ceremony attended by over 400 people. In his summary report, Li Chuwen pointed out that after the training, participants recognized the necessity of firmly standing on the ‚Äúpeople‚Äôs position‚Äù and clearing the harmful influence of the ‚ÄúChristian position.‚Äù Each participant also recognized the importance of self-criticism and ideological learning, while further solidifying their understanding of the significance of the Three-Self Movement.

  1. Adjustment of Colleges and Nationalization of Church Schools 1952 (Xing Fuzeng 2012b: 189-210)

In the early years of the CCP‚Äôs establishment, the approach towards the old education system (particularly private education) was one of ‚Äúprotection and maintenance, strengthening leadership, and gradually transforming.‚Äù In August 1950, the Central Committee of the Communist Party issued ‚ÄúGuidelines on Catholic and Protestant Issues,‚Äù reaffirming that church schools were ‚Äúprivate enterprises‚Äù and that the government would treat them equally under the principle of ‚Äúbalancing public and private interests.‚Äù The government set forth requirements: (1) church schools must include political courses as mandatory; (2) church-run higher education institutions could offer religious electives; (3) no exhibitions or mass meetings promoting or opposing religion should be held at schools; (4) student believers and non-believers should not attack each other over faith issues but should unite to oppose imperialist agents.

By the end of 1950, as the anti-American aid to Korea movement deepened, waves of accusations against imperialism for leveraging church schools for cultural invasion swept across the country, leading to vigorous mass movements within church schools. The central government‚Äôs policy on church schools also entered a new stage, with local campaigns against cultural invasion by American imperialism focusing on church schools. By the end of December, both China and the U.S. had frozen each other‚Äôs assets and public and private deposits, prompting the CCP to use the new context of the anti-American aid to Korea movement to accuse American imperialism of cultural invasion in China. Beginning in January 1951, all levels of church schools were fully involved in the wave of the anti-American patriotic movement.

From January 16 to 22, 1951, the Ministry of Education held a conference on ‚ÄúHandling Foreign Subsidized Higher Schools.‚Äù The attendees included leaders from the Education Departments or Cultural and Educational Committees of East China, Central South, and Southwest administrative divisions, as well as 85 representatives, including chairpersons, principals, teachers, and students from 19 higher education institutions receiving foreign subsidies.At the meeting, Minister of Education Ma Xulun pointed out that church schools were significant activities in the cultural invasion of American imperialism. In response to the United States freezing Chinese assets, the State Council decided to reclaim national educational sovereignty, transforming universities that previously received American subsidies into schools managed by the Chinese people themselves. He criticized church schools as an important avenue for America‚Äôs ‚Äúcultural invasion,‚Äù asserting that they disseminated toxic ideas that promoted admiration for America, fear of America, and glorified the decaying ‚ÄúAmerican way of life,‚Äù seeking to undermine Chinese national culture and harm the souls of Chinese youth. Lu Dingyi, Deputy Director of the State Council‚Äôs Cultural Education Committee, characterized the nature of the issues being addressed as a serious ‚Äúpolitical struggle against American imperialism.‚Äù

Following the conference on managing foreign-subsidized higher education institutions, the Military and Political Committees of East China, Central South, and Southwest convened meetings in February 1951 to address issues concerning secondary schools. By the end of 1951, the Ministry of Education completed a summary report on the handling of schools that received American subsidies. The overall evaluation of church schools in the report was that they were ‚Äúa cultural invasion base set up by imperialists under the guise of religion in China.‚Äù

In the autumn of 1952, the Ministry of Education began large-scale adjustments to higher education institutions, resulting in the dissolution of all church universities, which merged with other institutions. From then on, church universities became a historical relic in China. Simultaneously, the Ministry of Education issued directives to take over all private primary and secondary schools nationwide by 1954. The ‚Äúnationalization‚Äù of private schools marked the formal conclusion of the transitional phase of the old educational system in China. However, the national takeover of private schools was in reality a monumental undertaking involving vast resources. As a result, the plan, originally intended to conclude in 1954, was extended until 1956 before it was essentially completed.

  1. Movement to Accuse Christian Historiography (Xu Yihua 2004: 14-18; Xing Fuzeng 1996: 80-81; 2005: 212-219)

As early as the end of 1951, the renowned Chinese church historian Wang Zhixin conducted self-criticism regarding his book ‚ÄúOutline of the History of Christianity in China,‚Äù published in 1940. He indicated that his understanding of Anglo-American imperialism was ‚Äúvery vague‚Äù and that there were many imperialist ideological toxins within the text, even falling into reactionary views. Therefore, he ‚Äúrequested all those who possess this book to ruthlessly destroy it, to prevent the continued spread of this ideological toxin.‚Äù

From September to November 1953, Wang Zhixin published his research on ‚ÄúMaterials on the History of Chinese Christianity (Church History)‚Äù in ‚ÄúXie Jin,‚Äù addressing topics such as Christianity and unequal treaties, church incidents, and the Taiping Rebellion. In his writings, he criticized imperialism for using Christianity to invade China, asserting that the activities of missionaries in China ‚Äúare directly proportional to imperialist aggression against large nations.‚Äù Thus, he argued that ‚Äúthe history of Christianity‚Äôs missionary endeavors in China over the past 140 years is essentially the history of imperialist aggression against China.‚Äù

Despite Wang Zhixin‚Äôs attempt to apply the perspective of imperialist aggression to explain Chinese church history, the response was again critical. In February and March 1954, ‚ÄúXie Jin‚Äù published several articles criticizing Wang‚Äôs ‚Äúposition‚Äù in historiography as seriously problematic. Critics believed that while Wang acknowledged in his writings the fact that Western missionaries utilized Christianity to conduct aggression, he ‚Äúdid not articulate it adequately.‚Äù Furthermore, Wang appeared to view the courageous anti-imperialist actions of the Chinese people‚Äîthe church incidents‚Äîas mere ‚Äúchurch difficulties,‚Äù suggesting that they were due to ‚Äúthe ignorance of the common people and exclusivism among local officials.‚Äù This reflected Wang Zhixin‚Äôs insufficient awareness of the iron-clad reality that imperialism used religion as a tool for invasion, as well as his failure to clarify the intertwined nature of Christianity and unequal treaties. In an atmosphere of ‚Äúanti-imperialist‚Äù learning within the church, the publication of these articles inevitably produced negative effects and created considerable confusion among believers.

Due to the discussions concerning the issue of imperialism using Christianity for its aggression, the matter was not merely an academic discussion but rather a statement of political stance from the outset. Consequently, Wang Zhixin quickly responded, acknowledging his insufficient understanding and inappropriate wording. He reiterated, ‚ÄúIf it is said that these two articles of mine intentionally employed oblique language to criticize while covertly supporting, or that I aimed to secretly insert a leg into the conversation, I can firmly deny any such intention or malicious intent. After three years of study, with the help of my peers, I can now admit my understanding of anti-imperialism and patriotism and the Three-Self Reform has continuously improved.‚Äù In addition to Wang Zhixin, Lin Yongyu, the editor of ‚ÄúXie Jin,‚Äù also conducted a review, admitting that when addressing the history of imperialism using Christianity to invade China, if one does not genuinely stand firm on the people‚Äôs position and truly grasp scientific historical perspectives and methods, then even with a ‚Äúmountain of materials,‚Äù one cannot produce a true ‚Äúhistorical narrative.‚ÄùFrom 1953 to 1955, ‚ÄúXie Jin‚Äù published several critical articles concerning the history of Chinese Christianity, primarily assessing the conduct of missionaries (such as the Morrison family, Bishop Burge, Li Lezhi, Li Timoteo, and Pi Zhuwen). Additionally, Tang En compiled a series of historical materials under the theme of ‚Äúlong-term control by American imperialism‚Äù covering institutions like China Christian University, China Christian Theological Seminary, and biblical initiatives.

In 1957, Wang Zhixin, using the pseudonym ‚ÄúEr Wang,‚Äù discussed the early independence movements of Chinese Christianity. He distinguished between the ‚Äúold Three-Self‚Äù and the ‚Äúnew Three-Self,‚Äù evaluating the independence and contextualization movements of the church before and after 1949, along with the Three-Self Patriotic Movement, which sparked debate on how to assess the independence movement. Critics argued that he acknowledged the ‚Äúold Three-Self‚Äù while forgetting the fundamental people‚Äôs perspective, suggesting that the evaluation should differentiate between ‚Äúfalse Three-Self‚Äù and ‚Äútrue Three-Self,‚Äù as the independence and contextualization movements prior to 1949 were merely tools for imperialism to carry out ‚Äúinsidious and malicious aggression against China.‚Äù In March 1958, the editorial office of ‚ÄúTianfeng‚Äù officially concluded this debate. They ultimately published a response by Wang Yi to close the discussion and publicly released a document titled ‚ÄúReference Materials on the Issue of Self-Governance,‚Äù which had been compiled by the Secretariat during the 1954 National Christian Conference in China. Although the editors stated they ‚Äúdid not intend to conclude this discussion‚Äù as historical research can continue to be explored and debated, Wang Yi‚Äôs and the document‚Äôs content fully reflected the official stance and tone on the matter.

Wang Yi aimed to differentiate between imperialist missionaries and Chinese believers who led and participated in the independence movement. While he condemned the imperialist crimes of missionaries who invaded China and manipulated the Chinese church, he also recognized that the independence movement reflected the demands of Chinese Christians for autonomy in the early 20th century. He even suggested that the issue of ‚Äúchanging labels‚Äù should not be generalized. He superficially evaded whether the ‚Äúold Three-Self‚Äù necessarily equated to ‚Äúfalse Three-Self,‚Äù only asserting that there were elements of ‚Äúfalse independence‚Äù that had been utilized by missionaries within independent churches. Another distinction Wang sought to make was regarding elements in independent churches that had been labeled as counter-revolutionary. Thus, counter-revolutionaries like Ni Tuosheng and Jing Danying were considered false independent traitors; however, within their churches, there remained a large mass of misled believers who should not be indiscriminately dismissed. Old China, still under the dual pressures of imperialism and feudalism and lacking the leadership and support of the Chinese Communist Party, saw that the achievements of the ‚Äúold Three-Self‚Äù could not be compared with those of the ‚Äúnew Three-Self.‚Äù

The evaluations of historical materials and several issues related to the history of Chinese Christianity in the 1950s can be viewed as the outcome of the official attempts to establish a politically correct historical orthodoxy after 1949, described by Xu Yihua as a ‚Äúhistorical accusation movement.‚Äù The issues involved‚Äîhow to assess missionaries, missionary movements, independence movements, and contextualization movements‚Äîremain contentious topics within the discourse on the history of Chinese Christianity on the mainland.

  1. The Three Major Testimonies and Ten Major Tasks 1956 (Luo Weihong 2014: 712-716)

In January 1956, Mao Zedong convened the highest state council meeting, directing the reinforcement and expansion of the people‚Äôs democratic united front, to unite all possible forces. Following Mao‚Äôs suggestion, the Central Committee of the CCP held a special meeting regarding intellectuals, and Zhou Enlai‚Äôs speech during the meeting fully demonstrated the party‚Äôs attitude toward winning over intellectuals. Subsequently, Mao delivered two important speeches in April and May (on ‚ÄúTen Major Relationships‚Äù and ‚ÄúLet a Hundred Flowers Bloom, Let a Hundred Schools of Thought Contend‚Äù), reaffirming the CCP‚Äôs concern for economic development and its determination to grant intellectuals greater freedom.

In this relatively relaxed political climate, the China Christian Three-Self Patriotic Movement Committee convened its second plenary (expanded) meeting in March 1956, with a total of 249 attendees and representatives, exceeding the number present at the 1954 National Christian Conference. The meeting added 13 new committee members and 4 standing committee members, including newly participating spiritual faction individuals. Several ‚Äúspiritual faction‚Äù members spoke sequentially, including Jia Yuming, Yang Shaotang, Sun Hanshu, Zuo Furu, and Yan Jiale.

Wu Yaozong delivered a report on the ‚ÄúChina Christian Three-Self Patriotic Movement,‚Äù proposing the well-known ‚ÄúThree Major Testimonies and Ten Major Tasks.‚Äù The so-called ‚ÄúThree Major Testimonies‚Äù included: the testimony of achieving the Three-Self of the Chinese Church, the testimony of participating in socialism, and the testimony of striving to defend world peace. In explaining the ‚Äútestimony of the Three-Self,‚Äù he stated, ‚ÄúChristianity in China is derived from foreign sources. While spreading the gospel is indeed a good thing, many pious and devoted believers from abroad have offered numerous prayers and contributed significant financial resources to the work of evangelism.‚ÄùHowever, unfortunately, imperialism exploited missionary activities to conduct insidious maneuvers, tightly linking the church system with colonialism‚Ä¶ After years of effort, the Chinese church was able to break free from the ties of the missionary system and colonialism, and Chinese Christians could unite to establish and thrive within their own churches, embarking on the paths of self-governance, self-support, and self-propagation. He pointed out that the fundamental significance of the Three-Self Patriotic Movement is for Chinese believers to manage their own churches, which ‚Äúdoes not equate to the Chinese church severing its spiritual fellowship with Christians from other countries.‚Äù Opposing colonialism and the invasions of colonial states is not against ‚Äúbelievers and people from other countries.‚Äù Therefore, believers from various national churches are members of one body, with Christ as our common head. The fully autonomous, self-supporting, and self-propagating Chinese church enables Chinese believers to bear better witness to the gospel of Christ. He reiterated, ‚ÄúWe place great importance on the rich heritage of Christianity over the past two thousand years; this valuable legacy is proof of the work done by the Holy Spirit among believers throughout different eras and regions.‚Äù A fully realized Three-Self Chinese church can also contribute to the spiritual fellowship of Christians worldwide.

What Wu articulated regarding the ‚ÄúThree-Self‚Äù was no longer centered on struggle and accusation but restored more religious meaning. The relationship between Christianity and imperialism did not possess the intrinsic linkage that characterized the accusation movement, but rather returned to a level of being ‚Äúexploited.‚Äù More importantly, in explaining the mission of the ‚ÄúThree-Self‚Äù at that time, there was no previous tendency towards exclusion; he recognized the evangelistic efforts of foreign Christians driven by religious sentiments throughout history and affirmed the spiritual exchanges between the Chinese church and universal Christians, as well as the Chinese church‚Äôs inheritance and development of the two-thousand-year church tradition and heritage, thereby imparting new content to the ‚ÄúThree-Self.‚Äù

The report on the ‚ÄúTen Major Tasks‚Äù also demonstrated the vision and direction for church development. Among the ten tasks, three were of a political and social nature (actively participating in socialist construction, striving for world peace, and promoting patriotic education), while the other seven were church-centered: consolidating and expanding unity, mutual respect, improving church organization, establishing self-support promotion committees, advancing self-propagation studies, promoting publications, meeting the spiritual needs of believers, strengthening theological research, and cultivating talents. Wu‚Äôs propositions regarding ‚Äúbuilding the church‚Äù indeed brought a fresh and relaxed atmosphere to the Chinese church, which had experienced years of political movements and had been raised under the banner of ‚Äústruggle.‚Äù

  1. Overseas Connections of Chinese Christianity 1956-1957 (Matsutani 2016: 175-193; Zhao Tianen, Zhuang Wanfang 1997: 94-95)

After the establishment of the CCP, missionaries from Europe and America withdrew from China, marking the end of the missionary era. Simultaneously, under the political climate of anti-imperialist patriotism, the Three-Self Movement not only aimed to sever ties between the Chinese church and imperialism but also fully implemented the accusation movement against Western missionary agencies, labeling them as tools of imperialism in the invasion of China. In this context, the overseas connections of Chinese Christianity were essentially at a standstill. In 1952 and 1955, the British Archbishop of Canterbury, Hewlett Johnson, and a delegation from the British Quakers visited China at the invitation of the Committee for the Defense of World Peace.

As for external visits, they were limited to Eastern European communist countries; for example, in 1952 and 1954, Liu Liangmo and Bishop Zheng Jianye were invited to attend representative conferences by the Christian Democratic Union in East Germany.

Starting in 1956, the Three-Self Patriotic Movement Committee made significant breakthroughs in international connections. In terms of visits, in March 1956, the national Three-Self Movement invited Gustav Nystrom from the Evangelical Alliance of Switzerland, Josef L. Hromadka from Czechoslovakia, Bishop Jano Peter of the Reformed Church of Hungary, and Bishop Rajah Manikam from the India Lutheran Church to visit China. In May 1956, Bishop Hall of the Hong Kong Anglican Church visited China. Between May and October, visits were received from officials of the Japan Christian Women‚Äôs Association, the principal of the St. Guildford Children‚Äôs Home of the Hong Kong Anglican Church, the secretary-general of the United Churches of India, and representatives from the Anglican Church of Australia. From February to September 1958, delegations from the Theological Seminaries of Hamburg University in West Germany, the Japan Christian Visiting Group, and the Hungarian Christian delegation visited China.As for overseas visits, in the summer of 1956, Bishop Ding Guangxun attended the preparatory meeting for the Lambeth Conference of the worldwide Anglican Communion in the UK and later participated as an observer in the meeting of the Central Committee of the World Council of Churches (WCC) held in Hungary. During this period, the general secretary of the World Council of Churches suggested that Ding change his status from observer to representative, which Ding did not agree to. The WCC had been accused of being a tool of imperialism during the accusation movement. After the meeting, Ding traveled to West Germany to attend the World Christian Student Federation meeting (with Zhao Fusang and Huang Peiyong also participating). In September, the Chinese Christian delegation went to East Germany to participate in the eighth representative conference of the Christian Democratic Union. In 1957, a representative of the China Christian Church visited India, and later a delegation from the national Three-Self Movement (including Ding Guangxun, Huang Peiyong, Shen Derong, and Chen Jianxun) made a reciprocal visit to Hungary, where the Reformed Church of Hungary conferred an honorary doctorate on Ding Guangxun at the Berenyi Seminary. In November, the Chinese Christian delegation visited Hungary at the invitation of the Hungarian Christian Association.

It is evident that from 1956 to 1957, prior to the Anti-Rightist Campaign, the national Three-Self organization made efforts to restore some connections with the universal church, both in terms of hosting visits and making outbound trips. In alignment with Wu Yaozong‚Äôs ‚ÄúThree Major Testimonies and Ten Major Tasks‚Äù regarding ‚Äúpromoting fellowship and friendship among Christians and peoples of all nations,‚Äù the national Three-Self organization established the China Christian International Liaison Committee in May 1956. The countries and organizations they engaged with were no longer limited to Eastern European countries; they even attempted to connect with overseas denominations (such as the Anglican Church) and the very groups they had accused during the Korean War (the World Council of Churches). This indicates that under the relatively relaxed political atmosphere of 1956-57, Chinese Christianity was exhibiting breakthroughs.

However, following the Anti-Rightist Movement, the political landscape in China tilted left again, and the aforementioned space for engagement dissipated, leading to the inability of the China Christian International Liaison Committee to continue its operations. In 1958, Bishop Ding Guangxun canceled his plans to attend the Lambeth Conference of the Anglican Communion in London.

  1. Publication of ‚ÄúHoly Light‚Äù 1957 (Wang Weifan 2013: 2)

According to a resolution from the second national conference in 1954: ‚ÄúUnder the common goal of opposing imperialism, patriotism, and love for religion, we aim to promote the unity of all believers within the Christian church, further advance the Three-Self Patriotic Movement, and adopt the principle of mutual respect regarding differences in faith, systems, and rites among various churches.‚Äù At the end of 1956, Bishop Ding Guangxun suggested that ‚ÄúNanjing Three-Self‚Äù publish a devotional periodical. The publication invited Jia Yuming to name it ‚ÄúHoly Light,‚Äù with plans for it to be a bimonthly. The editorial team included Rev. Li Chenzhong (from the spiritual faction, a graduate of Hunan Bible School and former pastor of the Christian Church in Sucheng, Anhui Province) and Wang Weifan, a graduate of Nanjing Union Theological Seminary.

The purpose of ‚ÄúHoly Light‚Äù was to ‚Äúexpound the truth of the gospel of Christ; nurture the spiritual lives of brothers and sisters in various places; and provide materials for church workers for Bible study, devotions, sermons, and pastoral experiences.‚Äù The inaugural issue was published on March 1, 1957, with contributions from authors including Jia Yuming, Jiang Peifen, Yang Shaotang, Sun Hanshu, Han Peter, Cai Wenhao, and Li Chenzhong, most of whom had backgrounds in the spiritual faction. By 1957, there were about 5,000 subscribers.

However, starting in early summer 1958, the religious community in Jiangsu Province launched a campaign against rightists. The bimonthly ‚ÄúHoly Light‚Äù ceased publication after its seventh issue (February 1958). Nonetheless, the ‚ÄúEditor‚Äôs Note‚Äù in that issue did not express an intention to cease publication, continuing to call for subscriptions and submissions.

  1. Joint Worship 1958 (Luo Weihong 2014: 729-740)

The year 1958 marked a watershed in the development of the Chinese church. As Mao Zedong initiated the Great Leap Forward, signaling a transition towards a communist society, it also marked the advent of an era characterized by ‚Äúadventurism‚Äù and an extreme leftist approach. In an atmosphere of nationwide adventurous sentiment, the communist utopia seemed poised to be realized within Chinese society.

The existence of the Chinese Christian church, especially the multidenominational structure, was viewed as an impediment to the socialist path during the Great Leap Forward and faced severe impacts. In the latter half of 1958, ‚ÄúTianfeng‚Äù began characterizing the organizational structures of Chinese churches and church groups as ‚Äúessentially maintaining a semi-colonial appearance from the era of imperialist domination,‚Äù with ‚Äúthe legacy of imperialism being preserved intact.‚Äù This so-called semi-colonial appearance included: (1) the distorted distribution of churches; (2) sectarian opposition and schisms within the church; (3) the issue of agencies from the era of imperialist missions; (4) the reactionary control of churches.The article further states: ‚ÄúWe must thoroughly change the semi-colonial appearance of Chinese Christianity in the continuing and deepening process of socialist education movements.‚Äù Under this situation, the Three-Self organization implemented joint worship across the country under the guise of changing this ‚Äúsemi-colonial‚Äù appearance.

Zhejiang Province was one of the first regions to respond. Between April and May 1958, the Ningbo church proposed four rectification plans, advocating: (1) economic coordination and unified support; (2) unified arrangement of pastoral personnel; (3) unified management of church properties; and (4) the establishment of productive organizations where pastoral personnel could engage in production. The three major church institutions in Hangzhou (the Zhejiang Conference of the China Christian Church, the Zhejiang Diocese of the China Anglican Church, and the Zhejiang-Shanghai Baptist Council) also decided to conduct joint operations in August. In Wenzhou, a citywide expanded meeting of the Three-Self Movement was held from May 26 to 27, 1958, where it was decided to abolish the original denominational names, unify personnel, eliminate denominational distinctions, and unify economic management. In terms of faith traditions, baptism, immersion, anointing, and Sabbath observance were free, but communion was to be held monthly, advocating the use of multiple cups during communion. The meeting also resolved to begin joint worship on the first Sunday of July. During the Great Leap Forward, only six churches remained active in the city, including Chengxi Church (Methodist), Canghe Lane Church (China Christian Self-Governing Church), Huayuan Lane Church (China Inland Mission), Yongguang Church (China Jesus Church), Toushou Naolou Church (Seventh-day Adventist), and Xichengxia Church (Christian Gathering Place). All other churches ceased activities. After July 1958, as church unification progressed, only Chengxi Church served as the venue for Christian activities in the entire city.

The model of joint worship quickly spread from Wenzhou to various parts of the country. Grassroots congregations from different denominations adopted methods of uniting with other congregations in the same region, significantly reducing the number of grassroots congregations. For instance, in Shanghai, the number of churches decreased from 208 in 1950 to only 22 after the implementation of joint worship, and later further reduced to 8. In Beijing, the number shrank from 65 in 1949 to just 4 by 1958. In Jiangsu Province, by the end of 1957, there were 321 Christian churches open in the province, but by early 1959, this number dwindled to 83, with Nanjing‚Äôs larger churches consolidated from 23 to 7. In Sichuan Province, the total number of churches before 1958 was 134, which was reduced to 67 after the implementation of joint worship. In Qingdao, the number was cut down from 50 to 10, and then further down to 7. In Hangzhou, 28 churches were merged into 5, which later reduced to 1 as pastoral staff participated in training, and after the training, a total of 3 churches opened (which was later reduced to 2).

The implementation of joint worship effectively dismantled the denominational system and reinforced the Three-Self organization‚Äôs control over Christianity. Since the founding of the CCP, the national and regional frameworks of various denominations had remained unchanged, and the development of the Three-Self organization was aimed at replacing the leadership of these denominations with reformist figures. However, with the existing denominational structures still in place, the organizational autonomy of the denominations was maintained to a certain degree, and the national Three-Self organization found it difficult to completely interfere with the economic and personnel authority of each national institution. After the implementation of joint worship, denominational organizations ceased to operate, and all levels of the Three-Self organizations became the superior leading bodies of urban and rural churches. This comprehensive leadership of the ‚ÄúThree-Self‚Äù over the ‚Äúchurch‚Äù fundamentally transformed the denominational system of Chinese Christianity and reflected the party-state‚Äôs proactive use of the political situation to implement a more radical religious policy. This was also an inevitable outcome of the highly centralized and unitary leadership system of the state.

During the Great Leap Forward, Wenzhou became a pilot ‚Äúno-religion zone,‚Äù and policies aimed at eliminating religion were officially put on the agenda. The joint worship promoted across the country significantly reduced the number of grassroots churches, with many pastors being reassigned to work in factories. All of these actions undoubtedly formed part of the ‚Äúelimination of religion‚Äù policy.

  1. Political Movements and Christianity in the 1950s (Ying 2014)

Since the founding of the People‚Äôs Republic of China in 1949, the CCP has successfully consolidated its one-party dictatorship through a series of political and mass movements (suppressing counter-revolutionaries, resisting U.S. aggression and aiding Korea, ideological remolding, the Three Anti and Five Anti movements, purging counter-revolutionaries, and the Anti-Rightist Campaign). Simultaneously, economic transformations (such as land reform and collectivization) realized the state‚Äôs control over land and other agricultural resources. The state gained control and allocation rights over the vast majority of resources within society. For a social member to obtain the most basic conditions for survival, it was necessary to acquire the corresponding resources from the state, thus forming a highly unified total society. Under this model of integration, the party-state penetrated deeply and widely into grassroots social life at an unprecedented level and scope, causing local social integration to be subordinate to the political integration at the state level, achieving a high degree of consistency between the two.The effectiveness of this integration model, however, came at the cost of destroying social independence and autonomy. In other words, the ‚Äútotalism‚Äù that Zou Dong referred to, where the power of political institutions can intrude into and control various levels and sectors of society without limits, reached its peak during the Cultural Revolution but developed through successive political movements starting from the early years after the founding of the country in the 1950s.

In the 1950s, as China moved towards totalitarian politics, every political movement, regardless of its purpose or the target of its actions, inevitably affected the Chinese church because Christianity was viewed as a heterogeneous force associated with imperialist aggression. For example, during the early political movements of the CCP‚Äôs regime, land reform not only mobilized urban churches to participate in rural land reforms, expressing their support through direct involvement but also led to rural churches being occupied and church properties being confiscated under the accusation of being ‚Äúlandlords.‚Äù The anti-American aid to Korea movement targeted the fight against ‚ÄúAmerican imperialism‚Äù and made Christian churches and their associated endeavors a focal point for liquidating and denouncing American ‚Äúcultural invasion.‚Äù Although the ideological reform was primarily aimed at intellectuals transitioning from the old society, Christian individuals were also required to learn to transform their thoughts through criticism and self-criticism, thus purging the ‚Äúreligious toxins‚Äù of imperialism. The Three Anti Campaign focused on ‚Äúanti-corruption, anti-waste, and anti-bureaucracy,‚Äù targeting cadres in government agencies, while the Five Anti Campaign referred to ‚Äúanti-bribery, anti-tax evasion, anti-theft of state assets, anti-quality deterioration, and anti-stealing national economic secrets,‚Äù primarily aimed at capitalists. However, large figures in Christian endeavors were also accused of violating the ‚ÄúFive Anti‚Äù campaign. Moreover, the Three Anti and Five Anti campaigns became intertwined with ideological reform, evolving into various forms of ‚Äúideological big tigers.‚Äù These movements were interconnected and conducted through intense mass mobilization methods (such as accusation meetings), aiming to expose and denounce class enemies (American imperialist lackeys, bad elements, and big tigers).

The mobilization methods mentioned continued to be applied in political movements after the mid-1950s, including the purge of counter-revolutionaries in 1955 and the Anti-Rightist Campaign (or Socialist Education) in 1957-58. Within these nationwide political movements, Christianity was inevitably required to purge counter-revolutionaries and rightists hiding within the church.

During the Great Leap Forward in 1958, Christianity had to fully cooperate with the transition to a communist society and undergo comprehensive changes. During this period, churches across various regions actively engaged with the party, and pastors were characterized as members of the exploiting class; even religious thoughts were temporarily classified as ‚Äútoxins.‚Äù In this context, joint worship further dismantled the denominational system of churches and, under the guise of merging, closed numerous churches nationwide.

Key Theological Issues

  1. Cleansing the Poison of Imperialism (ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û2012b: 127-130, 136-140; Ying 2022; Wickeri 1990: 133-146)

In January 1951, Âê≥ËÄÄÂÆó wrote in „ÄäÂ§©È¢®„Äã that the issue of ‚Äúself-propagation‚Äù is not about ‚Äúwho propagates,‚Äù but rather about ‚Äúwhat is being propagated.‚Äù If having Chinese people propagate the Gospel signifies the completion of ‚Äúself-propagation,‚Äù then the problem is clearly solved. Most Western missionaries have already retreated, and the task of spreading the Gospel has naturally been passed on to Chinese believers. However, Âê≥ firmly believed that the issue of self-propagation is not that simple. His concern was: What should Chinese believers ‚Äúpropagate‚Äù? If what is being propagated by Chinese individuals is still the same set of beliefs originating from the West, then the Chinese church remains deeply influenced by the poison of Western imperialism. Therefore, he said: ‚ÄúIf we truly want to achieve ‚Äòself-propagation,‚Äô Chinese believers must discover the treasures of Jesus‚Äôs Gospel for themselves, break free from the shackles of Western theology, reassess escapist thinking, and create a theological system of their own; only then can we express the true spirit of Jesus‚Äôs Gospel within the context of the new China.‚Äù

Âê≥‚Äôs interpretation of self-propagation indicates that the precondition for reasserting the ‚Äútrue spirit‚Äù of the Christian Gospel in the new China is to cleanse the influence of imperialist poison on Christianity. The ‚Äúpoison‚Äù mainly involves two aspects: (1) Reformism; (2) Supersessionism.

Regarding reformism, it mainly targets the social gospel movement related to the reform of Chinese society. The Christian Youth Association can be said to represent this aspect. On July 27, 1951, the National Council of the China Christian Youth Association and the National Council of the Young Women‚Äôs Christian Association held an accusation conference, where Âê≥ËÄÄÂÆó accused American imperialism of using ‚Äúreformism‚Äù to invade China through the youth association. What is reformism? Âê≥ stated, ‚ÄúAny approach that prettifies the surface, evades class struggle, and opposes the use of revolutionary means to overthrow the old social system is reformism.‚Äù He pointed out that the greatest task of the Chinese revolution is to overthrow the three major enemies: imperialism, feudalism, and bureaucratic capitalism. However, the youth association not only did not oppose these three enemies but also attempted to ‚Äúdeceive the masses‚Äù through public education, rural services, and labor welfare, thus ‚Äúblur the Chinese people‚Äôs perception of the dominance of these three major enemies.‚Äù The young women‚Äôs association representative ÈÑßË£ïÂøó also indicated during the accusations that the youth association advocated a ‚Äúthird way,‚Äù attempting to propose ‚ÄúChristian social democracy‚Äù outside of capitalism and communism. This assertion aims to ‚Äúdivide the revolutionary forces of the colonized people‚Äù and foster accomplices for imperialism.

Another figure, ÂäâËâØÊ®°, who became a backbone of the national Three-Self Movement, stated in a January 1952 seminar on the Christian ideological transformation that reformism seeks to ‚Äútransform society through gradual legal means, without revolution,‚Äù which is essentially ‚Äúreactionary.‚Äù Because it ‚Äúdissipates revolutionaryÂäõÈáè, numbs the people‚Äôs resistance to reactionary rule, and prolongs the lifespan of reactionary domination.‚Äù

Âú®Êñá‰∏≠Ôºå‰ªñÈ¶ñÂÖàÈòêÈáäÂü∫Ë¶Å‰∏ª‰πâ‰∏éÁé∞‰ª£‰∏ª‰πâÁ•ûÂ≠¶ÁöÑÁüõÁõæÔºåÁÑ∂ÂêéÂÜç‰ª•Âê¥ËÄÄÂÆóËëó‰Ωú‰∏∫‰æãÔºåËØ¥ÊòéÁé∞‰ª£‰∏ª‰πâÂØπ‚ÄúÂü∫Ë¶ÅË¶ÅÈÅì‚ÄùÁöÑ‚ÄúÂê¶ÂÆö‚ÄùÔºà‰∏ç‰ø°Ôºâ„ÄÇÊçÆÊ≠§ÔºåÊåáÂá∫‚ÄúËøôÂ∞±ÊòØÁé∞‰ª£Ê¥æÁöÑ‰ø°‰ª∞ÔºÅËøôÂ∞±ÊòØÁé∞‰ª£Ê¥æÁöÑÁúüÁõ∏ÔºÅÊàëÁß∞‰ªñ‰ª¨‰∏∫‚Äò‰∏ç‰ø°Ê¥æ‚ÄôÔºåÊòØ‰∏çÊòØÂÜ§Êûâ‰ªñ‰ª¨ÔºüÊòØ‰∏çÊòØÂØπ‰∏ç‰Ωè‰ªñ‰ª¨Ôºü‚ÄùÈíàÂØπ‚Äú‰∏ç‰ø°Ê¥æ‚ÄùÔºåÁéãÊòéÈÅìËøõ‰∏ÄÊ≠•ÊåáÂá∫‚ÄúÂΩºÊ≠§Â∞äÈáç‚ÄùÁöÑÂéüÂàôÂπ∂‰∏çÈÄÇÁî®ÔºåÂõ†‰∏∫‚Äú‰ªñ‰ª¨‰∏ç‰ø°ËÄ∂Á®£Ôºå‰ªñ‰ª¨‰∏çÊòØÂü∫Áù£ÂæíÔºõ‰ΩÜ‰ªñ‰ª¨‰º™Ë£ÖÂü∫Áù£ÂæíÔºåÊ∑∑Âú®Êïô‰ºöÈáåÈù¢ÔºåËÆ≤‰∏Ä‰∫õ‰ººÊòØËÄåÈùûÁöÑËôöÊûÑÁöÑÈÅìÁêÜÔºåÂéªËø∑ÊÉë‰ø°ÂæíÔºåË¥•Âùè‰ø°ÂæíÁöÑ‰ø°ÂøÉ„ÄÇ‚ÄùËøô‰∫õ‰∫∫ÊòØÊïô‰ºö‰∏≠ÁöÑÁ™ÉË¥ºÔºåÊòØÊ∑∑ÂÖ•ÁæäÁæ§‰∏≠ÁöÑÊä´ÁùÄÁæäÁöÆÁöÑË±∫Áãº„ÄÇ‰∏∫‰∫Ü‚Äú‰Ωø‰ªñ‰ª¨‰∏çËÉΩ‰º§ÂÆ≥Á•ûÁöÑÁæäÁæ§‚ÄùÔºåÂøÖÈ°ªËµ∑Êù•ÂèçÂØπ‰ªñ‰ª¨„ÄÇ‚ÄúÂØπËøô‰∫õ‰∫∫Ë∞à‰∏ç‰∏äÂ∞äÈáçÔºåÊõ¥Ë∞à‰∏ç‰∏äÂõ¢Áªì‚Äù„ÄÇ

Êé•ÁùÄÔºå‰ªñÂèàÈ©≥Êñ•Â¥îÂÆ™ËØ¶ÔºåÊåá‰∏§Ê¥æÁöÑÂàÜÂà´ÔºåÁªùÈùû‚ÄúÂ§ßÂêåÂ∞èÂºÇ‚Äù„ÄÇËÄåÈíàÂØπ‰∏ÅÂÖâËÆ≠ÂëºÂêÅ‚ÄúÂõ¢Áªì‚ÄùÔºåÁéãÁöÑÂõûÂ∫îÊòØÔºåÂè™Â∫î‚Äú‰∏éÁúüÂÆûÊúâ‰ø°‰ª∞„ÄÅÁÉ≠ËØöÁà±‰∏ªÁöÑ‰∫∫Âêà‰∏Ä‚ÄùÔºåËÄåË¶Å‚Äú‰∏é‰∏ç‰ø°Ê¥æÂíåÊÅ∂ÂäøÂäõÂàÜÁ¶ª‚Äù„ÄÇ‰ªñÊòéÁ°ÆÂÆ£Â∏ÉÔºå‚ÄúÊàëÊòØ‚Äò‰ø°‰ª∞‰∏äÊúâÁùÄÊüêÁßç‰∫Ü‰∏çÂæóÁöÑ‰∏çÂêåËÄå‰∏çËÉΩÂõ¢Áªì‚Äô‚Äù„ÄÇ‰∏ç‰ªÖÂ¶ÇÊ≠§Ôºå‰ªñÊõ¥Ë¶Å‚ÄúÂ•âÊàë‰∏ªËÄ∂Á®£Âü∫Áù£ÁöÑÂêçÂêå‰ªñ‰ª¨ÊàòÊñó‚Äù„ÄÇ‰ªñÂèàÊâπËØÑË∫´‰∏∫Â±ûÁÅµÊ¥æÂç¥ÂèÇÂä†‰∏âËá™ÁöÑÊ±™Áª¥Ëó©ÔºåÈáçÁî≥Áé∞‰ª£Ê¥æ‰∏éÂü∫Ë¶ÅÊ¥æ‰πãÈó¥ÁªùÈùû‚ÄúÂ§ßÂêåÂ∞èÂºÇ‚Äù„ÄÇ
