Â∞ΩÁÆ°Èù¢ÂØπÂ∑®Â§ßÁöÑÊîøÊ≤ªÊâìÂéãÁîöËá≥Ë¢´ÊçïÔºå‰ªçÊúâ‰∏çÂ∞ëÁ•ûËÅå‰∫∫Âëò‰∏çÊÑøÂèÇÂä†Áà±ÂõΩ‰ºöÔºåÂπ∂‰∏éÊ¢µËíÇÂÜàÁª¥ÊåÅÂØÜÂàáÂÖ≥Á≥ª„ÄÇËá™ÈÄâËá™Âú£‰∏ªÊïôÊòØ‰∏≠ÂõΩÊïô‰ºö‰∏éÊïôÂª∑ÂÖ≥Á≥ªÂÖ¨ÂºÄÁ†¥Ë£ÇÁöÑÂØºÁÅ´Á∫øÔºå‰πüÊàê‰∏∫‰∏≠ÂõΩÂ§©‰∏ªÊïô‰ºöÊó•ÂêéÂàÜË£ÇÊàêÁà±ÂõΩÊïô‰ºö‰∏éÂú∞‰∏ãÊïô‰ºöÁöÑÈáçË¶ÅÂéÜÂè≤ËÉåÊôØ„ÄÇÊçÆ‰∏≠ÂõΩÂ§©‰∏ªÊïôÁà±ÂõΩ‰ºöÂèä‰∏ªÊïôÂõ¢ÁöÑËµÑÊñôÔºåËá™1958Âπ¥Ëá≥2000Âπ¥Èó¥Ôºå‰∏≠ÂõΩÂ§©‰∏ªÊïôÂ∑≤ÁªèËá™ÈÄâËá™Âú£‰∫Ü179‰Ωç‰∏ªÊïô„ÄÇ

Anti-Christian Leaders

  1. Zhou Enlai (Xing Fuzeng 2023: 106, 112; 2012: 24, 29-34, 39-40; MacInnis 1972: 24)

Zhou Enlai (1883-1976), originally from Shaoxing, Zhejiang, was born in Huai‚Äôan, Jiangsu. He studied at Nankai Middle School in Tianjin in 1913 and later attended Nankai University. He had short-term studies in Japan, France, and England, and joined a communist group during his time in France. During the first cooperation between the Kuomintang and the Communist Party, he served as the secretary of the Central Bureau of the Communist Party‚Äôs Soviet Area. During the War of Resistance Against Japanese Aggression, he was the director of the Eighth Route Army‚Äôs office in Chongqing. After the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China in 1949, he served as the Premier of the State Council (also Secretary of the Party Group), Chairman of the National Committee of the Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference, and Chairman of the Central Committee of the Chinese Communist Party. He passed away in January 1976.

Zhou Enlai was a key pioneer in religious work in the early years of the Communist Party‚Äôs founding. In June 1950, at the second Party Group meeting of the National Committee, he categorized religions in China into ‚Äúethnic religions‚Äù and ‚Äúpolitically connected‚Äù religions, with the former being Islam and Lamaism, and the latter referring to Christianity and Catholicism, which he stated were ‚Äúconnected to imperialism.‚Äù He emphasized that one must not underestimate the use of Catholicism and Christianity as tools of aggression by imperialists. Chinese religious practitioners and activities must ‚Äúsever ties with imperialism to block the plots of imperialists.‚Äù However, this struggle was primarily aimed at activities involving reactionary and counter-revolutionary elements, while the vast majority of believers remained a target for unity. When dealing with reactionary elements in the religious community, it was necessary to not ‚Äúinvolve general religious issues,‚Äù and he reiterated the importance of ‚Äúrespecting everyone‚Äôs freedom of belief,‚Äù stating that the government should protect churches and temples from occupation and destruction.

In March 1950, the First National United Front Work Conference was held in Beijing, establishing the basic principles for work on Catholicism and Christianity after the founding of the nation. Zhou Enlai pointed out that the central policy was ‚Äúto sever ties with imperialism,‚Äù and ‚ÄúChinese religions should be handled by Chinese people.‚Äù While opposing imperialism, care should be taken to avoid ‚Äúentangling religious belief issues.‚Äù

In May 1950, Zhou Enlai met with Christian figures three times. During the second conversation on May 6, he addressed the concerns raised by church leaders, particularly regarding government policies on foreign missionaries and overseas donations, pointing out that ‚Äúforeign missionaries can easily become unwitting tools of imperialism,‚Äù hence church organizations should not hire more. However, the government did not require missionaries currently in China to leave immediately. On the issue of overseas donations, he stated that in principle, ‚Äúfundraising from abroad should not be continued,‚Äù but also advised not to ‚Äúblindly exclude‚Äù donations as long as there were no strings attached. He simultaneously pointed out that the main obstacle faced by Christianity in China was its ties to imperialism. He made it clear that the reason for Chinese people‚Äôs opposition to Christianity is inseparable from Christianity‚Äôs ties to imperialist aggression over the past hundred years. He reiterated that ‚Äútoday, American imperialism still attempts to use China‚Äôs religious organizations to undermine the People‚Äôs Republic of China.‚Äù Therefore, the government required religious organizations to clear up and sever ties with imperialism, establishing self-governing, self-sustaining, and self-spreading churches.

Zhou Enlai was fully aware that most church leaders found it difficult to accept the accusation of Christianity‚Äôs ties to imperialism. Therefore, in a third conversation lasting over four hours on May 13, he frankly stated that whether the government accepted Christianity depended on whether both sides could cooperate politically. He clearly proposed that theists and atheists, idealists and materialists could politically cooperate: ‚ÄúClearing imperialist influences from the religious sector does not mean that everyone in the religious sector has been used as tools of imperialism. Individually, one might feel they have not been used, but imperialism subjectively has its demands; they use religious organizations, which is a fact. The vast majority of believers may also be inadvertently used. We must clarify this point. Once this issue is clarified, it will only benefit the church.‚Äù Zhou Enlai reaffirmed that the government did not aim to eliminate religion but to ‚Äúremove the bad apples, a small number of lackeys, and Judases from the religious sector.‚Äù He did not hide the government‚Äôs bottom line: ‚ÄúWe need to expand our united front, and its boundaries depend on whether ties with imperialism, feudalism, and bureaucratic capitalism are severed.‚Äù ‚ÄúOnce you establish your political stance, you will not be discriminated against; only this way can there be a way out.‚Äù Zhou Enlai‚Äôs remarks led to the emergence of Wu Yaozong‚Äôs Three Self-Reform Declaration and the subsequent signature campaign.

In June 1950, at the Third Plenary Session of the Seventh Central Committee of the Communist Party, Zhou Enlai reiterated the need to help religious figures ‚Äúsever ties with imperialism‚Äù and support them to ‚Äúmanage their own religions, perform and sing on their own, without foreign missionaries.‚ÄùIn July, Zhou Enlai instructed the Cultural and Educational Committee under the State Council to establish a ‚ÄúResearch Group on Religious Issues,‚Äù led by Lu Dingyi, the deputy director of the committee, with Shao Quanlin, the deputy secretary-general, serving as the group leader. The main task of the group was to provide recommendations to the State Council regarding the situations involving Catholicism, Christianity, and Buddhism.

On December 29, the State Council approved several documents during its 65th executive meeting, including the ‚ÄúDecision of the Central People‚Äôs Government State Council on Handling Cultural and Educational Relief Agencies and Religious Groups Receiving American Subsidies‚Äù and the ‚ÄúRegulations on the Registration of Cultural and Educational Relief Agencies and Religious Groups Receiving Foreign Subsidies and Investment.‚Äù Zhou Enlai pointed out in the executive meeting, ‚ÄúIn the past, we envisioned that it would take another three to four years to completely eliminate the remnants of American imperialism from China. However, the recent announcement by the United States to freeze our assets in their territory has given us a very favorable opportunity to accelerate the clearance of American imperialism‚Äôs residual influence in our country. Now, we are announcing this order, which is a severe blow to American imperialism.‚Äù

  1. Lu Dingyi (Xing Fuzeng 2023: 112-116; 2012: 79-85)

Lu Dingyi (1906-1996) was from Wuxi, Jiangsu. In 1922, he enrolled in Shanghai Nanyang University and joined the Communist Party in 1925. After graduation, he devoted himself to revolutionary work, mainly engaging in the Communist Youth League and propaganda efforts. In 1937, he served as the head of the Propaganda Department of the General Political Department of the Eighth Route Army, and in 1945, he became the Minister of the Propaganda Department of the Central Committee. After the founding of the People‚Äôs Republic of China, he served as the Minister of the Propaganda Department of the Central Committee (1949-1952; 1954-1965), Deputy Director of the Cultural and Educational Committee of the Central People‚Äôs Government (1953-1954), and Secretary of the Party Group of the State Council‚Äôs Cultural and Educational Committee (1949-1952). During the Cultural Revolution, he was criticized and even imprisoned. He was rehabilitated in 1979 and served as an advisor to the Central Committee‚Äôs Propaganda Department in 1980. He passed away in May 1996.

The religious control system in the early years of the Communist Party‚Äôs founding centered on the Religious Affairs Committee established in January 1951 (later renamed the Religious Work Committee) within the Party, mainly led by the Propaganda Department and assisted by the United Front Work Department. On the government side, in July 1950, the Cultural and Educational Committee under the State Council established a ‚ÄúResearch Group on Religious Issues,‚Äù led by Lu Dingyi, mainly responsible for work concerning Catholicism, Christianity, and Buddhism (Islam and Tibetan Buddhism fell under the jurisdiction of the State Ethnic Affairs Commission). Lu Dingyi, as the Minister of the Propaganda Department and Deputy Director of the Cultural and Educational Committee (also Secretary of the Party Group), was the primary leader overseeing Christian and Catholic affairs in the Central Committee until 1954.

From January 16 to 25, 1951, the Second National United Front Work Conference was held in Beijing, focusing on united front work in the context of the Korean War. Lu Dingyi spoke about work concerning Christianity and Catholicism, stating that it was essential to decisively eliminate the cultural invasion of American imperialism in China, describing this as part of the ‚Äústruggle against imperialism.‚Äù To achieve victory in the anti-imperialist struggle, he suggested that ‚Äúin addition to launching a general mass anti-imperialist movement, we must also win over Catholics and Christians.‚Äù He candidly pointed out that these believers had long been influenced by American imperialists, and mobilizing them would be a difficult task. He specifically reminded cadres to approach the issue from a ‚Äúpatriotic perspective,‚Äù advising against starting with claims of the church being used by imperialism, which would make believers ‚Äúfeel uncomfortable and hard to accept.‚Äù Instead, he proposed to first clarify the principles of patriotism before pointing out that there were ‚Äúbad elements‚Äù within the church, which would make it easier for them to accept. Lu emphasized that the church in China ‚Äúwill not be quickly eliminated‚Äù and that even ‚Äúweakening‚Äù it would face significant challenges, asserting that ‚Äúone cannot implement dictatorship over religion.‚Äù Therefore, the current focus of religious work should be to make the church ‚Äúpolitically and organizationally sever ties with imperialism.‚Äù He stressed that there is a conflict between idealism (religion) and materialism, and that the emphasis now is on requiring religions to cut ties with imperialism, with the government needing to support the reform movement within Christianity to attract believers away from the influence of imperialism and achieve the goal of transforming the church.

From April 16 to 21, 1951, the State Council‚Äôs Cultural and Educational Committee held a ‚ÄúMeeting on Handling Christian Organizations Receiving American Subsidies.‚Äù Lu Dingyi stated at the meeting that it was ‚Äúa struggle‚Äù aimed at eliminating the cultural invasion influences of American imperialism in China. Chinese Christians must stand with the government on the common political foundation of patriotism. He repeatedly emphasized how American imperialism historically and currently used Christianity to conduct cultural invasion, and he specifically criticized the ‚Äúapolitical‚Äù stance of Christians, pointing out that Christians must assist the government in exposing counter-revolutionaries within the church.He hoped that Chinese believers would actively participate in the three major movements and eradicate thoughts of fear, admiration, and flattery towards the United States, establishing feelings of hatred, disdain, and contempt towards American imperialism. At the meeting, Lu Dingyi explicitly called for Christianity to launch a denunciation campaign.

From November 27 to December 16, 1953, the Second National Religious Work Conference was held. The meeting was presided over by Lu Dingyi, the Minister of the Propaganda Department of the Central Committee and Deputy Director of the Cultural and Educational Committee of the State Council. The conference summarized the work related to Christianity in the country from 1950 to 1953 and formulated work guidelines and tasks for the future.

  1. Zhang Zhiqi (Shanghai Local Chronicles; Zhang Zhiqi 1957: 52-77; 1958a; 1958b; 1958c: 2102-2103; MacInnis 1972: 23)

Zhang Zhiqi (1911-1983) was from Hanyang, Hubei. He joined the Communist Party Youth League in 1927 and became a member of the Communist Party in 1929. After 1935, he worked in Shanghai and participated in various patriotic organizations. In 1946, he organized and led crowds in Shanghai for an anti-civil war peace march. In 1947, following the establishment of the Central Committee‚Äôs Shanghai Bureau, he served successively as the Secretary of the Cultural and Industrial United Front Committee of the Shanghai Bureau, Secretary of the County Workers‚Äô Committee, and Secretary of the Incitement Workers‚Äô Committee. After the founding of the People‚Äôs Republic of China, he held various positions including member of the Standing Committee of the Central South Bureau of the Central Committee, Secretary-General of the Central South Military and Political Committee, and Deputy Director of the Central National Affairs Commission.

From August 1954 to January 1965, Zhang Zhiqi served as the Deputy Minister of the United Front Work Department of the Central Committee. After the anti-rightist campaign, Zhang aligned his understanding of united front work with the Party‚Äôs line. In his 1957 article ‚ÄúClass Contradictions and Internal Contradictions Among the People,‚Äù he stated that while China was mainly still in a situation of ‚Äúinternal contradictions among the people,‚Äù ‚Äúclass contradictions had not yet been completely resolved, and class struggle had not ended.‚Äù There remained ‚Äúa considerable number of people‚Äù opposing the Communist Party of China and even attacking it. In the ideological realm, the ‚Äúcontradiction between proletariat and non-proletariat thoughts‚Äù was still ‚Äúa relatively prominent contradiction.‚Äù If not handled properly, it could further escalate into confrontational struggles. Therefore, it was necessary to encourage moderates to ‚Äúlean left‚Äù or even ‚Äúmake a remarkable leftward shift.‚Äù

Shortly thereafter, in 1958, he attempted to lead the united front with class struggle in his essay ‚ÄúThe People‚Äôs Democratic United Front is a Special Form of Class Struggle,‚Äù stating that ‚Äúsocialist revolution must use struggle to eliminate the bourgeoisie,‚Äù and that the ‚ÄúPeople‚Äôs Democratic United Front‚Äù was ‚Äúa special method for engaging in class struggle‚Äù and ‚Äúa special weapon for advancing the socialist revolution and consolidating the dictatorship of the proletariat.‚Äù The united front was a combination of unity and struggle, aimed at ‚Äúconstantly achieving transformation.‚Äù Essentially, it emphasized ‚Äúpeaceful transformation,‚Äù specifically through a ‚Äúredemption policy‚Äù (that is, allocating benefits economically and politically) and educational methods to encourage self-transformation, characterized by ‚Äúboth tension and relaxation,‚Äù which required ‚Äúa long-term struggle process.‚Äù He was confident that a ‚Äúnew favorable situation‚Äù had been created, ‚Äúaccelerating the pace of socialist transformation‚Äù to achieve ‚Äúa socialist society without class exploitation.‚Äù

If class struggle had not yet concluded, the united front became a ‚Äúspecial form‚Äù of class struggle. How would the Communist Party deal with religious issues? In 1958, he published an article titled ‚ÄúAtheists and Theists Can Cooperate Politically and Walk the Socialist Path‚Äù in ‚ÄúPhilosophical Research.‚Äù On the surface, it seemed that atheists could collaborate politically with theists, but in reality, the article expressed how he understood the relationship between religion and ‚Äúclass contradictions.‚Äù It is evident that after the anti-rightist struggle, the Communist Party was grappling with the complexity of religious issues in the context of the struggle between ‚Äútwo roads‚Äù: socialism and capitalism. (1) Under the internal contradictions among the people, it did not mean that ‚Äúthe religious issue had no class contradictions among the people currently; or that antagonistic contradictions no longer existed.‚Äù In navigating the ‚Äúsocialist revolution,‚Äù specifically during the struggle between the two roads, the situation of the religious community remained very complex; (2) this was reflected in believers‚Äô insufficient political awareness of socialism. Meanwhile, the upper-level figures ‚Äúwho genuinely uphold anti-imperialism, love for the country, support for the socialist path, and the leadership of the Communist Party were still a minority.‚Äù There were many in the religious community who, although they ‚Äúmight accept the leadership of the Communist Party and walk the socialist path,‚Äù were still in a neutral political position, ‚Äúharboring varying degrees of skepticism toward the Communist Party and socialism or having conflicting sentiments.‚ÄùHowever, the serious issue is that within the religious community, there are still individuals who not only ‚Äúhave not clearly drawn the line between themselves and imperialism and counter-revolutionaries, but some have even openly expressed a firm unwillingness to sever ties with imperialism (especially the Vatican), opposing the socialist new China and the leadership of the Communist Party.‚Äù In this regard, the contradictions manifested are ‚Äúantagonistic contradictions, that is, enemy-versus-us contradictions.‚Äù These ‚Äúbourgeois reactionary rightists in the religious community‚Äù often disguise themselves in religious garb and are utilized by ‚Äúimperialists, remnant counter-revolutionaries, landlord class members, and rich peasant elements‚Äù to struggle against the Chinese people through the issue of religion. Furthermore, Catholicism and Christianity have originally been tools of imperialist invasion, and imperialism is still unwilling to accept its defeat in China. The threat of imperialism to China still exists, so their struggle against the Chinese people is long-term. ‚ÄúWe must continue to work hard to completely rid religion of the influence and control of imperialist forces and hostile classes, transforming the religious issue entirely into internal contradictions among the people.‚Äù

At that time, the socialist education movement in the religious community (i.e., the anti-rightist struggle) was in full swing. Zhang Zhiqi raised the issue of ‚Äúbreaking‚Äù and ‚Äúestablishing,‚Äù indicating that this movement, while ‚Äúaimed at breaking the political stance of capitalism among religious individuals and establishing a political stance of socialism,‚Äù did not ‚Äúrequire religious believers to abandon their faith.‚Äù He acknowledged that Marxist-Leninists could ‚Äúfirmly stand on a socialist political stance‚Äù without needing ‚Äúevery supporter of the socialist political system to be a Marxist-Leninist,‚Äù and that they could be allowed to retain different worldviews in terms of ideology and to keep their respective religious beliefs. In this publicly published article, he addressed criticisms that the Communist Party‚Äôs ‚Äúreligious policy is a deception‚Äù and that ‚ÄúCommunists want to eradicate religion.‚Äù Thus, despite the strong flavor of ‚Äúclass struggle‚Äù expressed in the text, he still affirmed the importance of the policy of religious freedom.

However, Zhang‚Äôs remarks at internal meetings conveyed a different message. In May 1958, the Communist Party held a professional meeting on religious work, where Zhang Zhiqi delivered a speech that communicated the central government‚Äôs basic stance on religious work to the religious cadres. He pointed out that religion, as ‚Äúa superstructure leftover from the old society,‚Äù although it had lost its foundation after the elimination of the exploiting classes, would not be ‚Äúimmediately eliminated‚Äù and could still ‚Äúprolong its ideology for a considerable period.‚Äù However, ‚Äúgenerally speaking, religion is unlikely to develop and will gradually weaken, and we should consciously facilitate its gradual weakening.‚Äù The so-called ‚Äúconsciously facilitate‚Äù means to implement reforms of the religious system.

Then, would it contradict the policy of religious freedom for the government to lead the reform of religion? In the current political situation, how should the policy of religious freedom be understood? Zhang Zhiqi emphasized that religious freedom, in addition to promoting freedom of religion, also includes ‚Äúthe freedom to promote non-belief‚Äù and ‚Äúthe freedom to promote anti-religion.‚Äù His understanding of ‚Äúprotecting religious freedom‚Äù is that it does ‚Äúnot help religion develop, but is aimed at politically uniting the masses of believers to work with the working people to develop productivity and advance politically.‚Äù Zhang candidly pointed out that to achieve the ‚Äúelimination‚Äù of religion, one must first promote its ‚Äúweakening.‚Äù ‚ÄúToday‚Äôs protection is for tomorrow‚Äôs elimination. Protecting religious freedom is to create conditions that prompt differentiation within the religious community, allowing them to use their own hands to transform themselves in a manner of self-reformation, ultimately achieving the elimination of religion.‚Äù

Anti-Christian Organizations

  1. Chinese Communist Party (Ying 2014: 884-901; MacInnis 1972; Yang 2012: 65-72)

The Chinese Communist Party is a revolutionary party with a strong ideological orientation. Both before and after the establishment of the nation, it insisted on transforming Chinese society according to revolutionary ideals and goals (communism), while also making adjustments to meet the needs of realpolitik. As a party that believes in materialism and atheism, how to handle religious issues has been an unavoidable topic in its revolutionary nation-building process. From a worldview perspective, the CCP adheres to the opposition between materialism and idealism, viewing religion as the ‚Äúopiate of the people‚Äù and its ultimate demise, but it also establishes unity and struggle against religion based on real political needs, as well as the means to achieve the end of religion. Thus, religious work has always been an important aspect of the CCP‚Äôs united front work, with judgments regarding unity and struggle made according to the political line and needs of different eras.

Reviewing the history of the CCP since its founding in 1921, religious struggle has become one of the fronts of the youth movement, student movement, and mass movement. The newly formed Chinese Communist Party actively intervened in the non-Christian movement in 1922 and infiltrated the Young Men‚Äôs Christian Association. It can be said that Christianity was the first religion the CCP made arrangements for and engaged with, using ‚Äúcultural invasion‚Äù as an important narrative and weapon in the anti-imperialist struggle. From the 1930s to the 40s, during the anti-Japanese resistance to the Nationalist-Communist civil war, the CCP emphasized uniting patriotic religious figures while fighting, expanding the united front according to the needs of realpolitik.

After the founding of New China, with the complex development of both internal and external situations, the CCP‚Äôs judgments on the situation of social revolution, the balance of power between friend and foe, the internal contradictions of the people, and changes in internal power struggles all influenced its religious policies. From the ‚ÄúNew People‚Äôs Democracy‚Äù that was emphasized in the early years to the comprehensive launch of the anti-American and aid Korea movement after intervening in the Korean War, the party engaged in a fierce struggle against imperialism (especially American imperialism) and various mass political movements closely related to it (land reform, ideological transformation, Three Anti and Five Anti campaigns). It can be said that between 1949 and 1953, although Christianity became one of the five recognized religions, it still needed to undergo innovation in the anti-imperialist struggle. Although the CCP proposed a distinction between religion and politics (imperialism), how to define the ‚Äúrunning dogs‚Äù and ‚Äútoxins‚Äù of ‚Äúimperialism‚Äù during the process of ‚Äúpoliticization‚Äù and radicalization of mass movements easily led to the problem of excessive targeting. However, in 1953, the CCP also began to realize the issues at hand and thus reaffirmed the goal of ‚Äúunity.‚Äù

In 1953, the CCP announced the end of the new democratic phase and gradually transitioned towards constructing a socialist society. However, concerning the question of how to eliminate the exploitative classes while walking the socialist path, it opposed using ‚Äúdirect elimination‚Äù methods, advocating instead for a ‚Äúgradual transformation‚Äù approach. Minister Li Weihan of the United Front Work Department emphasized that one should not attempt to overturn religion through atheistic propaganda, asserting that ‚Äúthe elimination of religion can only be possible when humanity has eliminated classes and greatly enhanced its ability to control nature.‚Äù In this context, the CCP understood ‚Äúeliminating religion‚Äù as a natural outcome of achieving a socialist society (i.e., natural demise). During the transitional period, religious work still focused on unity and transformation, with struggle as a supplementary aspect. The national Christian conference in 1954 proposed ‚Äúexpanding unity,‚Äù which can be seen as a concrete expression of this direction.

However, when ‚Äúexpanding unity‚Äù faced resistance from the spiritual faction, the CCP viewed it as an object of struggle and, through the anti-revolutionary campaign of 1955, regarded the anti-Three Self religious issue as a counter-revolutionary political issue. As a result, in 1955 and 1956, it successively dismantled the counter-revolutionary groups of Wang Mingdao and Ni Tuosheng in Christianity, and the counter-revolutionary group of Gong Pinmei in Catholicism. After the anti-revolution struggles, it successfully forced the spiritual faction opposing the Three Self Movement to join the patriotic camp.

In 1956, the political line of the CCP experienced a more relaxed adjustment, and Mao Zedong declared that ‚Äúlarge-scale, storm-like mass class struggles were basically over,‚Äù yet ‚Äúclass struggle had not completely ended,‚Äù thus proposing the direction of ‚Äúcorrectly handling contradictions among the people.‚Äù Against this backdrop, religion was seen as a contradiction among the people rather than that of hostile classes. At the same time, the CCP put forward the slogan ‚ÄúLet a hundred flowers blossom, let a hundred schools of thought contend,‚Äù leading to a relatively open environment for Chinese Christianity between 1956 and 1957.However, following May and June of 1957, the CCP‚Äôs judgment of the situation reversed to the anti-Rightist struggle, and the line of class struggle again dominated the CCP‚Äôs religious policy. The expansion of the anti-Rightist campaign became a large-scale political liquidation of Chinese Christianity, with most of the originally united subjects classified as ‚ÄúRightists.‚Äù

In China in 1958, the contradictions of friend and foe from the anti-Rightist struggle continued, while the atmosphere of ‚Äúleap forward‚Äù towards a communist society further radicalized religious work, leading to a ‚Äúpromoting weakening‚Äù approach aimed at the ‚Äúdisappearance of religion.‚Äù Under the extremely ‚Äúleft‚Äù line, the Christian system received unprecedented shocks, with many church venues closed under the pretext of ‚Äújoint worship,‚Äù and religious clergy were labeled as the ‚Äúexploiting class.‚Äù The ‚Äúno-religion zone‚Äù experiment indicated that religion had become the final target the CCP intended to ‚Äúeliminate.‚Äù

Anti-Christian Events/Movements

  1. Christian Accusation Movement 1951-1952 (ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 1996: 68-72; 2012: 82-96; ÂäâÂª∫Âπ≥ 2010: 123-133; Wickeri 1990: 134-146; Bush 1970: 187-199; MacInnis 1972: 238-241)

After the Chinese Communist Party intervened in the Korean War, the Central Committee of the Communist Party instructed various regions on October 26, 1951, to carry out current affairs propaganda activities, emphasizing the need to unify public understanding of American imperialism, particularly to ‚Äúgenerally develop an attitude of hatred, disdain, and contempt towards American imperialism.‚Äù At the end of December 1950, a patriotic rally to resist America and aid Korea was held in Shanghai. At the meeting, Chen Qiyuan, Deputy Minister of the Ministry of Public Security of the Central People‚Äôs Government, pointed out that Christians must recognize the true nature of American imperialism as China‚Äôs ‚Äúnational hatred‚Äù and not believe that America is benevolent or charitable just because it established schools, hospitals, and relief efforts in China. All this work, as well as American missionaries, are tools used by American imperialism to conduct aggression under the guise of religion and Christianity. Therefore, Christians must consciously cooperate with ‚Äúthree views‚Äù (i.e., hatred, disdain, contempt), especially to ‚Äúhate‚Äù American imperialism.

The Communist Party has always been concerned about the imperialist ideological and cultural invasion activities. Guo Moruo, Vice Premier of the State Council, believed that because American imperialism continued ‚Äúto secretly use these organizations and groups to conduct its reactionary propaganda and activities‚Äù after the establishment of the new China, the government must eliminate its influence and ‚Äúfinally, thoroughly, forever, and completely end over a hundred years of cultural invasion by American imperialism against the Chinese people.‚Äù The Central Committee instructed various regions to ensure that cultural, educational, and relief organizations and religious groups receiving foreign funding would ‚Äúforever detach from the influence of imperialism‚Äù and must ‚Äúlaunch a mass anti-American patriotic movement,‚Äù exposing the imperialist invasion conspiracy, while also ‚Äúholding accusations and demonstrations against American imperialism,‚Äù revealing and combating a tiny minority of reactionaries.

From January 16 to 25, 1951, the Second National United Front Work Conference was held in Beijing, focusing on the situation of the anti-American and aid Korea movement. Hu Qiaomu pointed out at the meeting that the ‚Äúanti-imperialist upsurge‚Äù triggered by the anti-American and aid Korea movement in China greatly promoted work in various areas and that any form of imperialist influence in China should be ‚Äúrooted out.‚Äù From March 15 to 19, 1951, the First National Religious Work Conference was held in Beijing, defining the reform movements within Christianity and Catholicism as an important part of the anti-imperialist political struggle, primarily aimed at cleansing the influence of imperialism on Chinese culture.

Beginning in 1951, the Communist Party officially convened meetings in different areas such as education, publishing, religion, and relief to initiate work on the anti-imperialist patriotic struggle. On January 15, 1951, the Ministry of Education held a meeting on ‚ÄúHandling Foreign Subsidized Higher Education Institutions.‚Äù From February 12 to 16, the Ministry of Health held a meeting in North China on ‚ÄúHandling Hospitals Receiving American Subsidies.‚Äù From March 16 to 22, the General Administration of Publishing held a ‚ÄúChristian Publishing Conference‚Äù in Beijing. From April 16 to 21, the Education and Culture Committee of the State Council held a ‚ÄúMeeting on Handling Christian Organizations Receiving American Subsidies.‚Äù From April 26 to 30, the State Council held another ‚ÄúMeeting on Handling Relief Organizations Receiving American Subsidies.‚Äù

Although these meetings were convened under the pretext of ‚Äúhandling American-funded institutions,‚Äù Han Wenzao, a progressive figure in the Nanjing Youth Association, pointed out that one should not ‚Äúview the problem in a bureaucratic way,‚Äù only focusing on how to request the thawing of bank deposits, procedures, etc., or understand self-reliance from an organizational perspective. He reiterated that the fundamental spirit of the State Council was ‚Äúto finally, thoroughly, forever, and completely end the cultural invasion of American imperialism against the Chinese people over the past hundred years.‚Äù In other words, this was a political struggle within the Christian community to thoroughly cleanse American imperialism. For example, Minister Ma Xulun of the Ministry of Education criticized church schools at the ‚ÄúHandling Foreign Subsidized Higher Education Institutions Meeting‚Äù as an important avenue for American imperialist ‚Äúcultural invasion.‚Äù Lu Dingyi also pointed out at the meeting that the essence of handling related issues is ‚Äúthe political struggle against American imperialism,‚Äù but during the struggle, a resolute unification policy should be adopted to help religious adherents detach from the influence of imperialism. At the ‚ÄúHandling Relief Organizations Receiving American Subsidies Meeting,‚Äù representatives from attending organizations also made a painful accusation against the brutal crimes committed under the guise of ‚Äúrelief‚Äù and ‚Äúcharity‚Äù that harmed the Chinese people and Chinese children. The representative assembly issued a joint declaration, criticizing the crimes of various relief projects organized by American imperialists.The ‚ÄúMeeting on Handling Christian Organizations Receiving American Subsidies‚Äù held in April successfully mobilized Christian leaders to speak at the accusation conference, unveiling an important chapter in the Christian accusation movement. During the six days of the meeting, two days were dedicated to holding accusation rallies.   1. The Disintegration of the Jesus Family 1952 (Èô∂È£õ‰∫û 2004; ÁæÖÂÅâËôπ 2014: 676-679; Lian 2010: 64-84; Wickeri 1990: 160-162)

The Jesus Family was founded by Êï¨Â•†ÁÄõ in 1927 as a Chinese indigenous sect characterized by charismatic features, emphasizing a communal lifestyle (family). In 1949, there were a total of 104 ‚Äúfamilies‚Äù nationwide, with 12,000 believers. After the founding of the People‚Äôs Republic of China, the Jesus Family actively supported the Three-Self Patriotic Movement‚Äôs signature campaign, with 381 members from the Jesus Family featured in the first batch of 1,527 signatures from Shandong Province. At the Three-Self Patriotic Movement seminar in Tai‚Äôan in June 1951, Êï¨ was a member of the presidium, but during the meeting, accusations against the Jesus Family began to surface, and Êï¨ also made a self-accusation. More than 120 people signed up to make accusations, and finally, 60 took the stage to accuse.

After the accusation conference, it was decided to ‚Äúsplit the family‚Äù of the Jesus Family, restoring 138 households based on blood relations, which were categorized into agricultural, industrial, medical, educational, and production households, among others, and redistribute properties. By October 1952, the ‚Äúfamily splitting‚Äù was completed, leading to the declaration of the Jesus Family‚Äôs ‚Äúdisintegration.‚Äù The ‚ÄúÈ¶¨ËéäËÄ∂Á©åÂÆ∂Â∫≠‚Äù was also renamed ‚ÄúÂåóÊñ∞ËéäÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÂ†Ç.‚Äù The working group announced the ‚Äúend of imperialism and reactionary feudal forces‚Äô rule and exploitation of the È¶¨ËéäËÄ∂Á©åÂÆ∂Â∫≠.‚Äù Ultimately, Êï¨Â•†ÁÄõ was arrested by the government and imprisoned in Tai‚Äôan Prison.

With the disintegration of the ‚Äúold family,‚Äù various ‚Äúsmall families‚Äù also began their Three-Self Patriotic Movement studies, splitting their families and dissolving existing organizations. The Jesus Family became the first Christian sect to be comprehensively ‚Äúbanned‚Äù after the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China.

  1. Christian Counter-Revolutionary Group Case 1955-1956 (ÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û 2010: 97-147; 2005: 121-140; Wickeri 1990: 162-170)

In 1955, leftist literary theorist ËÉ°È¢® faced criticism for his independent views and was soon deemed by the Central Committee as part of a ‚Äústruggle against the ËÉ°È¢® counter-revolutionary group.‚Äù This nationwide anti-ËÉ°È¢® movement further evolved into a nationwide campaign to purge counter-revolutionaries. On July 11, the publication „ÄäÂ§©È¢®„Äã issued an editorial entitled ‚ÄúStrengthen Unity, Distinguish Right from Wrong,‚Äù which for the first time named and criticized ÁéãÊòéÈÅì, a Beijing Christian Church leader who publicly opposed the Three-Self Movement. In the early morning of August 8, ÁéãÊòéÈÅì and his wife were arrested at home, along with approximately 20 others in Beijing, È¶ôÂ±±, Èï∑Êò•, and Âª£Â∑û.   1. Wenzhou ‚ÄúNo Religion Experimental Zone‚Äù 1958 (ÊîØËèØÊ¨£ 2000: 41-42; Êú±ÂÆáÊô∂ 2011: 95-96; ËàçÁ¶æ 2015: 483-495)

During the Great Leap Forward, the national Three-Self organizations proposed to thoroughly change the semi-colonial appearance of Christianity by implementing ‚Äúunited worship‚Äù in August 1958. Prior to this, Wenzhou in Zhejiang Province had already decided to merge in a Three-Self expanded meeting held in May, planning to implement the merger from July. According to the regulations, all denominational names were to be canceled, personnel unified, with no distinction of sects, and property and finances centralized. Initially, among the 17 churches in the urban area, 6 were allowed to remain open, corresponding to the 6 original denominations: ÂüéË•øÂ†Ç (originally Methodist), ÊªÑÊ≤≥Â∑∑Â†Ç (originally Self-Reliance Association), Ëä±ÂúíÂ∑∑Â†Ç (originally China Inland Mission), Ê∞∏ÂÖâÂ†Ç (originally Jesus Self-Reliance Association), È†≠Ê¢≥ËÖ¶Â†Ç (originally Seventh-day Adventist), Ë•øÂüé‰∏ãÂ†Ç (originally Assembly).

The model of united worship quickly spread from Wenzhou to various parts of the country. Grassroots congregations from different denominations adopted a method of uniting with other congregations in the same region, significantly reducing the number of grassroots congregations.

According to Ê≤àÂæ∑Ê∫∂, a member of the national Three-Self Standing Committee, in the summer of 1958, the head of the Religious Affairs Bureau, ‰ΩïÊàêÊπò, mentioned to him in Shanghai the concept of ‚Äúfly-free cities‚Äù and ‚Äúfly-free countries‚Äù proposed in various places in Beijing during the Great Leap Forward. ‰Ωï believed that ‚Äúin terms of religion, could we create a ‚Äònation free of religious activities‚Äô throughout China?‚Äù Following this, ‰Ωï visited Wenzhou for inspection, and Ê≤à believed that the later ‚Äúleftist errors‚Äù regarding religion in Wenzhou were closely related to this visit. Ê≤àÊâÄÊåáÁöÑÔºåÊ≠£ÊòØ Wenzhou‚Äôs early implementation of united worship, and the subsequent establishment of the ‚ÄúNo Religion Experimental Zone.‚Äù

The Wenzhou ‚ÄúNo Religion Experimental Zone‚Äù materialized in Wenzhou‚Äôs subordinate counties: Âπ≥ÈôΩ, Ê∞∏Âòâ, Ê¥ûÈ†≠, ÁëûÂÆâ, and ËíºÂçó, where all religious activities were essentially halted during the Great Leap Forward. All churches were closed, rather than merged. In the urban area of Wenzhou, the initial 6 opened churches for united worship were further merged down to only ÂüéË•øÂ†Ç.

The summary report on religious work from Wenzhou in 1959 stated: ‚ÄúIn the city, one Catholic church, one Christian church, and one Buddhist temple will continue to be retained for proper religious activities, while all suburban churches have halted services indefinitely. No public interference will be conducted regarding household gatherings, nor will they be officially recognized; efforts will be made to monitor the situation, actively promote education and persuasion, and remain vigilant against disruptive elements spreading rumors‚Ä¶‚Äù

The Wenzhou ‚ÄúNo Religion Experimental Zone‚Äù reflects the beginning of the CCP‚Äôs policy to ‚Äúeliminate‚Äù religion during the Great Leap Forward. This policy would later be implemented nationwide during the Cultural Revolution.

  1. Suppression of the True Jesus Church 1958-59 (Inouye 2018: 208-210; ÈÑßËÇáÊòé 1993: 2-19)

During the Great Leap Forward, the True Jesus Church became one of the sects heavily targeted and impacted. The True Jesus Church is an indigenous Christian sect in China, founded in 1917. In March 1951, the True Jesus Church held its 12th General Assembly, where it formulized a reform program expressing a commitment to enhance political education and pursue aid for Korea. In April, Wei Yisai, as president, attended the ‚ÄúMeeting on Handling Christian Organizations Receiving American Subsidies‚Äù convened by the State Council and co-signed a joint declaration. In early 1952, Wei Yisai conducted a self-critique expressing his intent to participate in the Three-Self Movement. In March 1953, the General Assembly issued notices to various regions to study the Three-Self reform declaration, resulting in over 13,000 believers signing by September. In April, the True Jesus Church General Assembly Committee for the Three-Self Reform Movement Preparatory Committee was established, and the General Assembly moved its headquarters from Beijing to Wuhan, with the national president and vice-president (Ëî£Á¥ÑÁø∞ and ÂäâÂùáÂÆâ) elected as president and vice-president. During this period, Wei Yisai faced criticism.

In May 1958, Zhang Zhiyi, Deputy Minister of the United Front Work Department of the Central Committee, mentioned the need to eliminate the True Jesus Church during a specialized religious work meeting. This suggestion was quickly relayed to various provinces.On June 5, 1958, a specialized religious work conference in Sichuan Province addressed related issues and required local authorities to ‚Äúassess the situation.‚Äù In January 1959, the province decided to start in Chongqing, where the congregation was concentrated, operating under unified leadership from the party committee. A joint working group composed of the United Front, public security, and religious affairs departments was formed, followed by the establishment of cooperative groups at the county level for unified actions against the True Jesus Church. By the end of May, the campaign had essentially ‚Äúdefeated and discredited the True Jesus Church, achieving the expected objectives,‚Äù with the ‚Äústruggle reaching a conclusion.‚Äù For example, in Chongqing, among 135 key members of the district assembly, a total of 82 were targeted (16 arrested, 34 under control, 2 sent for labor re-education, 10 struggled against, and 20 faced public review and debate criticism).

In May 1958, the national Three-Self Movement began simultaneous criticisms of the True Jesus Church. After „ÄäÂ§©È¢®„Äã published reports on the ‚Äúillegal activities‚Äù of the True Jesus Church in Hubei, it emphasized, particularly in an ‚Äúeditorial,‚Äù that such ‚Äúcriminal activities against the Party, socialism, and the people‚Äù must not be tolerated. In June, following reports on the illegal activities of the True Jesus Church in Hanyang, Hubei, „ÄäÂ§©È¢®„Äã included a ‚Äúshort commentary‚Äù and reprinted an article from the Hubei Christian Socialist Education Study Committee denouncing the True Jesus Church. In July, „ÄäÂ§©È¢®„Äã published letters from readers at the True Jesus Church in Beijing, calling for a denunciation of the reactionary group led by Ëî£Á¥ÑÁø∞ at the General Assembly of the True Jesus Church.

During the Great Leap Forward, the True Jesus Church was the only sect specifically named and subjected to unprecedented political purging. This may be attributed to its tightly organized national structure and strong ‚Äúcharismatic‚Äù tradition. Additionally, the True Jesus Church‚Äôs ‚Äúexclusive‚Äù view of the church, asserting itself as the only ‚Äútrue‚Äù church, led to its classification as a detrimental element to unity, even though it participated in the Three-Self Movement, resulting in a comprehensive crackdown. After the implementation of united worship, all denominational organizations ceased operations, and congregations were required to merge with other sects. However, some scholars suggest that the True Jesus Church was still able to develop in an underground state.
